BOOKS OF THE DAY.
On receipt at Box 659, G.P.O., Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to
the price stated, any book in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australian address
“THE YELLOW ASTER,”
By Iota. 6s. ; posted, 6s. 6d.
HUTCHINSON & CO. 8vo., Cloth, 307pp.
When a book goes through a dozen editions in little more than as many weeks, the proof is pretty conclusive that it cannot be dull, at all events. And dulnesa
is the last reproach that can be flung at “A Yellow Aster.” It is “queer,” to be sure. Not many girls in Australia grow up incapable of flirtation, and prepared to offer
a merely experimental “ Yes ” when the fateful question is popped by an eligible partner. Exactly how queer and how entertaining this book is can only be found out
by personal perusal. Press notices hitherto have mainly suggested a charm without doing much to define it. And as the germ of “ A Yellow Aster ” was.found in
Australia, and the authors were for some time residents of Melbourne, the book has special interest for Australian readers. This edition is tastefully bound, and very
neatly printed.
“THE LAST SENTENCE,”
By Maxwell Gray. 3s. 6d.; posted, 3s. lid.
HEINEMANN’S COLONIAL LIBRARY. 8vo., Cloth, 311pp.
Many people will read this story with a great deal of pleasure and interest. “ The Silence of Dean Maitland,” by the same author, remains after many years a
prime favorite : and “ A Last Sentence ” is very similar in method and treatment. Several strong situations lead up to the climax, when a judge is called upon to-
sentence to death his own daughter, and here the average novel-reader will thrill and palpitate with excitement. A like incident, indeed, is strongly put in Lytton’s
“ Paul Clifford ” and Franzes’ “ Chief Justice ;” and the author has found it necessary in a preface to deny having read either book. But, if a rose be sweet, what does it
matter that others have plucked one as sweet before us? Anyhow, “Paul Clifford” is forgotten, and “The Chief Justice” has never been known enough to be remem
bered. “ The Last Sentence ” is bound to have a great vogue.
“THE PRISONER or ZEADA,”
By Anthony Hope. 2s. 6d.; posted, 2s. lOd.
MACMILLAN’S COLONIAL LIBRARY. 8m, paper, 310pp.
This story, to hand by the last mail, might easily have been told on the Thousand and Second Night. The hero suddenly finds himself masquerading as king of
a little German State during the indisposition of the rightful monarch, and the trouble that he has in defending his borrowed crown proves conclusively that there is
nothing in monarchy —in small German States. There is an adventure, with drawn revolvers and flashing sabres, on every page, and blood flows like colonial beer on a
navvies’ pay-day. Adventurous people whom unkind Eate has set to handle spades and flashing pens can read “ The Prisoner ” for a change.
“THE RUBIOOA,”
By E. F. Benson. 2s. 6d. ; posted, 2s. lOd.
BELL’S INDIAN AND COLONIAL LIBRARY. 8m, Paper, 394pp.
This is Mr. Benson’s second novel. His first, “ Dodo,” said to be a portrait of the lady who is making Mr. Asquith happy, came in the first flight of the rushing ■
throng which have exhibited London society as a leper asylum in evening dress. The lepers who cross “The Rubicon ” give reason to think that their doctors and nurses
have a lively time of it. The lady leper, having been persuaded into a loveless marriage, makes herself so disagreeable that the gentleman leper, who isn’t a bad sort of a
leper at all, is glad to do it with prussic acid, or something. Then the lady arranges things badly with the other fellow, and across the brook she goes. Of course, such a
book is bound to be more or less the rage. And nobody can claim to be really up-to-date in end-of-the-century literature unless he (she) has taken a psychological dive into
the depths and shallows of “ The Rubicon.”
“ LESS of the H’URBERYILLES,”
By Thomas Hardy. 2s. 6d. ; posted, 2s. lOd.
BELL’S INDIAN AND COLONIAL LIBRARY. 8vo., Paper, 520pp.
This is a cheap re-issue of a book adjudged three years ago to be a masterpiece of English fiction —a judgment which the lapse of time has strengthened and
confirmed. Thomas Hardy has no superior but George Meredith among contemporary novelists : with that noble exception he is peer of the highest. He is no mere
prattler of witticisms : his stories are not dummies for the display of a style filed and fitted to the ninth part of a hair; he writes for Fame and Time, not to gratify a
false taste of the fickle, momentary public. In “Tess” he has given us of his best. Conception is as strong as execution. No wonder that the book startled Mrs. Grundy
in her strongholds. The author deliberately challenges conventions and prejudices, and declares that purity is an ethereal essence of the spirit, not of the body : that a
wronged woman may stand on a level with her of the most sheltered virtue ; and that there are saner and wider views of sex than those held by the diseased minds of
monkish mystics and incorporated in traditional religion. This edition is well printed in large type, and has an excellent portrait of the author.
“REMINISCENCES of the GREAT MUTINY,”
By W. Forbes-Mitchell. 2s. 6d. -, posted, 2s. lOd.
MACMILLAN’S COLONIAL LIBRARY. 8vo., paper, 295pp.; with maps.
A glimpse of the Indian Mutiny from the inside. The average historian, writing, as a rule, long after the events which he describes, has a difficulty in
identifying himself with the men who have moved them. Do what he will, they remain mere puppets of his mind, things extern and often unintelligible. The author of
these Reminiscences, a sergeant in the 93rd (Sutherland) Highlanders, saw the events which he chronicles; and his unpretentious narrative has a vigour and a thrill which
more studied performances frequently lack. The chief episode dealt with is the Siege of Lucknow. Many personal anecdotes are interspersed ; and the book, as a whole,
is readable and informing.
“THE LAST of SIX”:
TALES OF THE AUSTRAL TROPIGS.
BY
ERNEST FAVENC.
With prefatory note by
Rolf Boldrewood.
PRICE : : Is.
Posted to any Australian
Address, Is. Sd.
“ The volume takes its name from one of the sketches, ‘ The Last of Six,’ though in point of merit they all stand pretty well on a par. The
author, who is familiar with life in Northern Queensland, writes with vigour about the doings of the whites and the natives in these faraway
parts. A strong vein of mysticism runs through his narratives, and there are ghosts in almost every chapter, and for those who are fond of
realistic work the volume will prove attractive reading.”—S.M. Herald, July 8, 1893.
“ No writer has given the world a more vivid description of Australia and its wilderness than Ernest Favenc, and the secret of this is that
he has, by many years’ experience of travel and bush life, acquired a knowledge of this continent which is possessed by scarcely any other writer.
He, too, possesses great descriptive powers. Whatever he puts pen to is certain to be good. In the stories under notice he has given us some of
his best work. That they will be widely read is certain.” —Sydney Mail, July 8, 1893.
“ They present with force and vividness, as well as with the accuracy arising from long contact and keen observation, striking pictures of
parts of Australia unfamiliar to most of its inhabitants ; they tell, sometimes with remarkable power, of incidents such as happen in wild places
when human hearts are stirred by strong passions, and of weird imaginings that move the minds of lonely men. * The Last of Six ’ is a terrible
tale of cannibalism, treachery, and revenge upon the dead, which in other hands might be gross and hideous, but which Mr. Favenc makes of
thrilling interest. ‘ The Haunt of the J inkarras ’ suggests Rider Haggard and his imitators to some extent, but is itself relieved from the
suspicion of imitation by faithful local colour and a grotesquerie of conception, in which Mr. Favenc here and there approaches Poe. This
characteristic is especially noticeable in * Spirit Led,’ which the great American might have written, * A Cup of Cold Water’ is, perhaps, the
best tale in a collection that should be widely read, and which forms a worthy addition to jpurely Australian literature.”—Daily Telegraph
July 15, 1893.
“‘The Last of Six ;’and other stories. By Ernest Favenc, (Sydney: The Bulletin Newspaper Co) This is a collection of short but
graphic sketches by Mr. Favenc, the well-known explorer, who has a rare collection of Australian legendary lore. All the stories here are good,
and we can heartily endorse the recommendatory notice by Rolf Boldrewood prefixed to this collection. The present issue forms the third of The
Bulletin series of Australian reminiscences.”—Australian Star, July 15,1893.

The Caligraph Writing Machine,
M
m
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“ I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that ths platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

UNLIMITED
Means {Vide Webster’s Dictionary)
“Having No Bounds-”
A Venture in Co-operation,
Now Placed Before the Public Depending
Upon Their Support, which Is Only Expected
If To Their Advantage.
Member ship, £1 Is, Per Annum.
MEMBERS can have any business done by
Unlimited, to best advantage and at lowest
rates, and on completion of the business will
receive half the amount earned by Unlimited.
Examples. The National Association of
Queensland paid the Unlimited Agency .£lB 15s.
for procuring a loan of .£750 for the Association
from the Bank of North Queensland, vide Tele-
graph, 14th June. By payment of .£1 Is. to
Unlimited they would now receive a refund of
.£9 7s. 6d., a net saving of .£8 6s, 6d.
A.B. paid the Unlimited Agency <£s for pro-
curing a loan of .£lOO. By payment of £1 Is. to
Unlimited £2 10s. would have been returned, a
saving of £1 9s. more than a year’s subscription.
Every member receives a Policy of Insurance
against accident in the New Zealand Accident In-
surance Company for £5O in the ease of death,
and 10s. per week in the case of disablement for
ten weeks, subject to ordinary conditions of the
company.
Every member is entitled to a discount on any
purchase above Is. made from any firm federated
with Unlimited. Such discount will be paid in
cash by Unlimited on the 15th of the month fol-
lowing the month of purchase on presentation of
receipts for payment. All receipts for payment
must be presented to Unlimited by the sth of the
month after their date, and will be returned witli
the payment.
Example. —You spend ss. with one of the
Federated Firms on the sth August. Most of the
firms now give you a receipt; if not offered a
receipt, ask for one. On or before sth September
present your August receipts to Unlimited. On
15th you will receive the discount in cash and your
receipts.
Note. —The discount being per cent., if you
spend £42 a year you must get value for your
subscription.
Every member will receive (for cost of postage
only when necessary) a Monthly Register of the
Federated Firms. Such Register will take the
form of a journal useful to every member.
The whole of the books, accounts and securities
will be audited by a professional accountant, and
a certified balance-sheet will be published in the
Journal every six months.
Unlimited is new, and there seems no more
likely way of progression than by means of sug-
gestions from members. Prizes will be offered
every month through the Journal for the best
suggestions in any way connected with the im-
provement of the venture.
I reserve the right to cancel any membership
upon conviction of anything derogatory to the
venture.
Note. —Loan Prize on page 14.
The First Issue of the Journal (5000 copies) free,
on application at the office.

Afternoon Tea a Specialty•
When A. G. Taylor’s old constituency of
Mudgee heard of his defeat at the poll for East
Sydney, some kindly spirits telegraphed him :
“ Your best friends here have no sympathy !
This result is damnable ! Weep!” It is always
one’s best friends who rub it in after this
fashion.
Ex-Chief Justice Stephen, of N.S.W.,
celebrated his 92nd birthday on Monday week, and
received numerous congratulations, including the
felicitations, delivered in person, of no less than
60 members of his family. The only non-relative
privileged to see the veteran on this occasion was
the irrepressible Mandarin Tart, who called in
full fig, accompanied by a big birthday-cake.
The Bulletin has noticed on more than one
occasion a somewhat grotesque coincidence : the
Draconian ex-Judge’s birthday seems, by some
strange fatality, to be selected by Australian
Governments as a day for hangings. True to pre-
cedent, they butchered a man at Melbourne last
Monday.
Dear Bulletin, —The lastnewsfromSamoa
described the native war as still fizzling, while
hungry soldiers stole R. L. Stevenson’s fowls.
Samoa evidently wants some more British Govern-
ment. At present it only costs her 74,000 dols. a
year for “law and order.” By-and-by she will
advance to the Fijian status, and feed a staff of
snobs and Govt, cricket team for <£74,000 a year—
five times as much. Down in Tonga, which once
called itself Tonga the Forbidden (Tonga-tabu) is
a constitutional Kingdom with independent native
administration, “on British models.” Marvellous
models, if one can judge by the imitation ! The
best imitation of a British model I saw in the
South Seas was a copy of a kilt.— Pohtemonnaie.
Dear Bulletin, —The “ loyal toasts were
duly honoured ” at Premier Reid’s banquet.
Bismarck called the people who yearn to cheer for
somebody who does not care a straw for them —
“ the hurrah canaille.” He ought to know. There
is no better occasion for a self-gratifying display
of loyalty than a death in any royal family. A
black ribbon round the arm indicates a kind of
distant relationship or intimacy with the deceased,
and gives an air of distinction to the mourner.
In 1888 I was at Singapore, when the Government
decided to mourn for the German Emperor.
Whenever I met a fellow with black ribbon round
his arm (and these were to non-mourners in the
proportion of about one to four) I could make cer-
tain of recognising the stamp of conceit and
stupidity on his face. Both kings and priests
found their empire on ignorance. Yours,
POLAOK.

Here is a Maoriland love-letter with a
faint tinge of poetry in it: —
My Dearest Marey,—l be very well any apply to inform
you that i be very well at present thank God and i hope
you be the same. Dearest marey ibe very sorry to here
how as that you dont like Wellington. As I shant be able
to look on yer Dear fase so often as i have done Dearest
marey pure and holey meek and lowly lovely rose of
Sharon. Dear marey i hant got now know particulars
moore to tell you at present but my sister thats married
have got such a nice little baby and i wish how as tiiat our
affairs was settled and we had such a nice little dear to.
Dearest marey i shall not be happy till then. Dearest
marey pure and holey meek and lowly lovely rose of
Sharon, sometimes i do begin to dispair as I am afraid
our not will never be tide. Dearest marey pure and holey
meek and lowly, lovely rose of sharon. Ibe a coming to
Wellington to-morrow to by the ring and you must come
to the station to meet me, and bring a piece of string the
size of your finger, and be sure you don’t make a mistake.
Dearest marey granny is going to give us a 51b note to by
such as a washing stand, fireirons, mousetrap, and soap,
and ye must wate until we can buy carpeting and glass,
crockeryware, and chiney. Dearest marey pure and
holey meek and lowly lovely rose of sharon, i be very
happy to tell you our old sow has got young uns last nite.
Dearest marey pure and holey meek and lowly lovely rose
of sharon so no more at present from your future and
loving husband.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
mund the ankle DU ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot or

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings gterliny—with postaye added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., andall Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully rend and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential .
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But ire desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission oj such MSS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 1, 1894.
The Woman’s Suffrage Experiment.
South Australia has fallen into line with
Maoriland on the Woman’s Suffrage question,
and the greatest political experiment of the
nineteenth century is so much the nearer.
In all the Australias, as well as in almost all
other civilised lands, Woman’s Suffrage may
be set down as inevitable, and even its most
irreconcileable opponents are ‘beginning to
realise the fact. Both the womanhood and
manhood vote became practically assured
when free and compulsory education was
instituted. It was only a question of wait-
ing until the first generation reared under
the new system had come to maturity.
Therefore, the Conservatives who bitterly
opposed compulsory education were wise in
their own narrow-minded day and genera-
tion. They realised that the dominion of
the classes was founded upon ignorance, and
on ignorance alone, and that the educated
but unpropertied man of the future would
flatly refuse to believe chat he was an in-
ferior animal, and unfit to have an equal
share in the management of the State,
merely because his propertied fellow-man
told him so. And it was equally inevitable
that the educated woman of the future
would refuse to believe in her own political
incapacity merely because man assured her,
on his own unsupported statement, that she
was incapable and would always continue so.
Education is the one influence which destroys
all reverence, undermines all dogma, and
scatters veneration to the winds. The poor
man lost his superstitious respect for the
rich man when he began to study animal
physiology, and found out what the rich man
was composed of. And woman lost a good
part of her faith in the overwhelming
superiority of man when she began to dissect
him in the medical schools and found out
what he was composed of. Also, both sexes
lost most of their reverence for royalty, and
the Church, and other fetishes, because they
began to analyse them and found that they
could not be seriously dissected, because they
were made of no ponderable substance what-
ever. /
# * •
Regarded as a matter of abstract justice,
there is no argument against female suffrage
which can bear close inspection. The
allegedly inferior sex is held responsible to
the law, therefore, according to all democratic
principles, it should have a share in the
making of the law. It has to supply its
share of the State revenue, therefore, it has
a right te a share in controlling the State
expenditure. It bears its full share in the
world’s labor, therefore it has every right to
a voice in deciding what manner of world it
will be. The only serious argument against
womanhood suffrage, regarded as a matter of
ethical justice, is that the work of national
■defence falls entirely upon man, and that the
fighting part of the nation should also be the
part which governs. But, considering that
not one male in a hundred does fight for his
country if he can possibly help it, this argu-
ment tells almost as strongly against one sex
as against the other. If it means anything
at all, it means that the non-military male
should be disfranchised as well as the non-
military female, and that the country should
be governed solely by the armed forces. And,
considering that these forces are generally
altogether disfranchised, and that the fight-
ing part of the male community is the part
which, above all others, is not allowed to
govern, it is evident that the argument was
never intended to be taken seriously. Ninety-
nine per cent, of the male population defends
its country merely by contributing- to war
expenditure, and as the female population
defends its country in exactly the same
manner there is very little perceptible
difference.
• * 9
The matter at issue is not the justice of
womanhood suffrage, which is sufficiently
apparent, but its expediency, concerning
which no one really knows anything. A few
small communities have adopted the prin-
ciple with the result, so far, that those which
were previously conservative have, on the
whole, continued to be conservative, and
those which were previously progressive
have remained progressive. But the experi-
ment has never yet been attempted on a
sufficiently large scale to furnish any definite
results. Women are, by common consent,
astonishingly and comprehensively ignorant
of politics, and at first they will, to a great
extent, be fooling with the political machine
very much after the manner of a child fool-
ing with a locomotive. This was inevitable,
for no one ever learns politics except by
actual experience. Of the male voters who
were enfranchised in England by the early
Reform Bills, an enormous proportion could
neither read nor write, many had a vague
idea that electoral reform meant a general
division of property, and a very large
number did not even know the names of the
existing political parties, and recognised
them vaguely as “blues” and “yellows,”
and as nothing more. The enfranchised
woman of to-day or to-morrow starts
from a far more advanced point than
the enfranchised male of not so very many
years ago. Also, she starts from the same
point as a largo, if not the larger, proportion
of the male voters of to-day, for the great
mass of the community has no definite
political opinions of its own ; it inherits its
views, in the first place, and changes or
modifies them later on as it is told to do by
newspapers, or by political orators. The
reason why every cause or party desires to
have an organ of its own, and why it sends
its most eloquent orators out to stump the
country and deliver addresses to all and
sundry, is simply that even the most
educated community has not got any further
than a rudimentary idea that all men are
equal ; it has still to be supplied with ready-
made ideas as to how to use its equality.
# # »
The male political atom, however, despite
his constant need for a supply of ready-made
opinions, has made great since
he first attained to power. If he is still
hazy about many things which he ought to
do, he has acquired—mostly by rote—a large
amount of valuable knowledge about many
things which he ought not to do, which
knowledge prevents him dropping back
into the slough of his old, hopeless ignor-
ance. It is claimed, however, that the
female political atom is incapable of such
advancement, that she is unprogressive by
nature, ignorant, lacking in originality,
devoid of initiative and merely gifted with a
faculty for imitation, that she is priest-ridden
by instinct and conservative by an ineradi-
cable tendency for which there is no remedy.
Most of these statements, and many more of
the same kind, were, and still are, assigned
by the propertied male as a reason why the
unpropertied male should not be entrusted
with the suffrage. In that case, as in the
one now under consideration, they rested
entirely on the bare assertion of an interested
party. They were falsified in the one
instance ; whether they will or will not also
be falsified in the other no one can foresee.
According to the medical science of to-day,
the female brain is less developed and shows
far less capacity than that of the male ; the
medical science of to-morrow may endorse
this view, or, again, it may decide, as it has
so often done before, that the science of
what will then be yesterday was merely the
reasoning of fools. The woman of the past
has contributed 90 per cent, less than her
fair proportion to science, art, literature,
and progress generally ; but whether that
was owing to natural incapacity or to
countless centuries of subjection and edu-
cational neglect, can only be decided
by a lengthened trial under more equal
conditions. She has supplied little that
was new, but whether that was because
she was incapable of originality, or because
almost every outlet for ambition was denied
her, there is r: o evidence to show. Also, no
one really knows, by any reliable proof,
whether woman is, to any notable extent,
more subject to priestly influence than the
male of her species, or, if she is, whether
the circumstance arises from natural ten-
dencies or from hereditary training, or, in
either case, whether the evil is any more
incurable in her case than in that of equally
priest-ridden man of a few generations ago.
Female suffrage is absolutely experimental;
it is the one political expedient of whose
ultimate consequences no one on this earth
knows anything at all. There are practically
no data to go upon. All that is known is
that the experiment is inevitable.
* • •
Womanhood suffrage has, in a feeble sort
of way, been classed with the standing jokes
of the period. Whether this has helped it
or retarded it is difficult to say ; probably it
has done neither. It has ranked with the
mother-in-law joke, and the subtle American
humor concerning the nigger and the pump-
kin, and the various weird conceptions
regarding the tramp, and the home-made
pie, and the young man and the girl at the
front gate, and the ancient harridan in
pants. The Shrieking Sisterhood which wants
the suffrage—next-of-kin to the Shrieking
Brotherhood which wants One-man-one-vote
—has passed into a proverb. Also, it has been
proved over and over again by incontestable
argument that women should not have the
suffrage—first, because they don’t want it and
would never use it; and, second, because they
want it so badly that they would presently
devote their entire time to politics, to the
neglect of all other duties. Both these latter
facts, by the way, were also proved in the
case of the unpropertied male voter when it
was first proposed to entrust him with the
franchise. But logic and alleged humor are
equally of no account in the case, for
the humor is feeble and stale, and the
logic has no definitely ascertained facts to
start from. All that is known is that woman-
hood suffrage is based on abstract justice ;
that it is obviously inevitable ; and that no
one knows what it will lead to. The Bul-
letin supports it because it is based on
abstract justice ; it acquiesces in it because
it is inevitable, and the person who refuses
to acquiesce in the inevitable is merely
shaking an impotent talon at the universe ;
and it inwardly objects to it because it leads
towards unknown and unforeseeable conse-
quences. If this attitude is rather less
coherent than it might be, the intricacy of
the problem is responsible.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“The Bulletin” Knows a Bank.
The report of the Q. Auditor General for 1892-3
contains these words :
The Bank of England advanced the Govt. £400,000 on
June 17, 1892, which was repaid by the sale of Treasury
Bills in Oct., 1892. Another advance of £600,000 was ob-
tained from the Bank of England on Deo. 20, 1892, and
was repaid from the proceeds of the sale of inscribed stock
in Feb. and March, 1893. Both these advances were ob-
tained to provide funds for the payment of the half-
yearly interest, and the latter should not have been re-
quired, there being sufficient funds to the credit of the
Govt, ip London to meet all liabilities, especially as a
draft for £300,000 was sent from Brisbane, which, how-
ever, was only credited by the Bank on Jan. 3, 1893.
This means in plain language that the Q. Govt,
had funds sufficient to pay its half-yearly
interest lying idle with the Queensland National
Bank, in London, It implies, in addition, that
.£300,000 was sent from Brisbane in time to
help to pay interest—for unless sent in time
there was no use in sending it at all. This
sum, however, was “only (mark the ‘only’)
credited by the bank on Jan. 3, 1893 ” —after
the interest had been paid. To pay the interest,
£600,000 was borrowed from the Bank of Eng-
land—for a consideration, of course. Obviously,
such a transaction could nob pay the Govt. ;
but the Q.N. Bank was saved from disbursing
£600,000. When the bank suspended payment
on May 13, 1893, there were £748,348 to Govt,
credit at its London office. This sum, or part of
it, was included in the £2,000,000 of Govt, moneys
locked up by Act of Parliament till July 1,
1899, and repayable then by 12 half-yearly
instalments. The bank thus retains £2,000,000
for six years, and then begins to repay it at the
rate of £333,000 a year, being charged per
cent, interest meantime—a figure which little
more than covers the cost of the money to
Government. The Government could have
readily found local authorities to take this
amount at 5 per cent, at least, and on quite
as good security. As every increase of debt
tends to lessen credit, the Govt., therefore,
weakened the credit of the province, not only
without drawing advantage from the loan, bub
even sacrificing its advantage to the necessities
of the bank—which, of course, lends at its dis-
count and overdraft rates of, say, 7 and 8 per
cent., the money borrowed on the credit
of the people, for which it pays per cent.
Now, who is the bank? A list filed at Q.
Supreme Court, on Feb. 28, 1894, contains the
names of 21 members of the Q. Legislature,
who were then shareholders of the Q.N. Bank.
Now, a wise provision of Parliamentary law
prevents any member from taking a contract or
office of profit under Parliament, unless in the
work of administration. Does it follow, then
that those members of the Queensland Legisla-
ture who are also shareholders of the Queens-
land National Bank would betray their public
trust if they influenced or consented to or re-
frained from opposing arrangements by which
the public funds might be used for the profit of
that bank ? Certainly not. Does it follow
that they are using their Parliamentary posi-
tions to the disadvantage of the taxpayers?
Certainly not. Does it follow that the finances
of Queensland are improperly administered by
a set of politicians for their own benefit?
Certainly not. To misquote Sheridan : “ The
Queensland Parliament is a family of great
versatility and uncommon honesty, and the
Q.N. Bank may be personified as a lady beauti-
ful in person, modest in her deportment, refined
in her sentiments, and of nice morality, split
my windpipe !”
The Artful Property-Tax.
Out of Melbourne’s chief Tory mouthpiece
comes a simple proposal to enforce direct taxa-
tion in Victoria in a way that would lead to the
largest possible amount of dissatisfaction. “If
stop-gap (!) taxation is to be imposed, we must
reiterate the belief that it would be better to
go to property than to income,” says the Argus.
After reiterating most of the Tory fictions con-
cerning the alleged difficulty in taxing personal
profits, the organ comes to the point that “ the
property-tax would cover a far wider area than
a restricted income-tax, and would excite much
less of class feeling.” In short, the property
impost would fall upon so many struggling agri-
culturists and impecunious owners of small
weatherboard cottages, and be met with so
many protests of inability to pay it from work-
ing-men out of billets, that the Argus might
presently describe a good principle as something
that had been put into practice and found bad.
Times out of number Capital has called atten-
tion to the happy state of the labouring land-
lord, and instanced his possession of property
as a proof that he had no excuse for
going on strike. But Capital no longer
begrudges the unemployed bricklayer his
dilapidated cottage and few square feet of
soil, seeing that the aforesaid properties may
serve to unite the classes and the masses in one
common complaint against the “injustice” of
direct taxation. Poverty may be Wealth’s
catspaw for pulling its sovereigns out of the
Treasurer’s fire. “By property, of course , we
mean not real estate simply and merely, but
property of any and every description—wealth
in all its realised forms,” says the Argus. Now,
the poor man’s own house and the few miser-
able sticks therein are the chief items of realised
wmalth that escape the income-tax obligation.
A prosperity, not a property-tax, is the matter
which Fat Man wishes to avoid, and prosperity
is represented by the financial income. The
unfairness of a property-tax suggestion is
beautifully expressed in the thought that it
“ would cover a far wider area thana restricted .
income-tax.” It would be no tax at all upon
the high-salaried civil servant whose extra-
vagance preserves him from property, and who
would be very justly reached by an income-tax.
But it would be a scandalous imposition upon
thousands of people whose incomes are little or
nothing per annum, and would result in the
political failure that the Argus clan is anxious
to bring about. There is no deceptive ring
about the words property - tax. They are
pleasant to democratic ears, for “ proputty ” is
associated with prosperity, unearned increment,
high living, and invitations to banquet at the
public expense. Whereas a representative
gaudy financier might spend every year
thousands of income money without contri-
buting a penny to the tax that pressed upon a
hundred of his poorly-paid employes.
A Question of Privilege.
One afternoon last week a tall parson was
summoned to the bar of the Victorian Legisla-
tive Assembly to justify a piece of one of his
sermons. The stalwart shepherd had men-
tioned from the pulpit that disgraceful indivi-
duals made their way into Parliaments and
were prepared to do anything mean and con-
temptible when they got there. A newspaper
report represented the gentleman as using the
words “ liars and profligates.” Clearly he had
said something derogatory to the dignity of the
’Ouse, and sb he was called to the bar for the
purpose of being bluffed, if possible, into a
recantation. Only a few members wished to
move in this dangerous matter at the outset.
By the time the parson was actually on the
spot the desire to demand an apology was
limited to a very select circle indeed, but the
gentleman having been summoned to the bar, it
was necessary to do something other than ask
him to take a drink. Therefore, Speaker
Thomas Bent read aloud to the parson the
newspaper report complained of, and asked him
if the report was correct. The parson replied
“It is not quite accurate,” and with that
Speaker Bent would fain have closed the pro-
ceedings. Bent, for his part, was perfectly
satisfied to know that the parson’s words were
not quite accurately taken down. He didn’t
enquire as to whether the real original sentence
had been hotter or milder than it appeared in
print. The parson might have said “ accursed
liars and profligates,” but it was all the same
to the Speaker as long as the report happened
to be incorrect in any particular. In fact,
Speaker Bent plainly showed that he was not
in favor of making the parson explain himself,
Also, Premier Patterson and other experienced
tacticians tried to get the House quit of the
accuser on the cheapest possible terms. After
a ludicrous wrangle, however, a section of the
Assembly managed to carry its point that the
parson must be called upon to say something
definite. Truly a great opportunity for a
reverend person who loves notoriety and is in
his own showing anxious to raise the moral
tone of the Legislature. Yet he didn’t say any-
thing definite. He read an indefinite statement
which spread his accusation over parliaments
in general, and repudiated the idea that he had
been thinking of the Vic. Assembly in parti-
cular. His statement finished thus —“ I was
thinking at the time, I may say, of such in-


stances as Jabez Balfour and cases like that ,
and of the financial frauds disclosed amongst
members of Parliament in Canada and in the
Panama scandals. With such instances as these
impressed upon my mind, I said that scoundrels
and blacklegs were too often returned to Par-
liament, not ‘liars and profligates,’ as reported.
That is all I have to say,” From which The,
Bulletin concludes that the shepherd’s pluck
was not fully equal to the emergency, nor was
his heart as faint as the House would have
liked to find it. He was allowed to retire with-
out cross-examination, and the Vic. Assembly,
having succeeded in making a profound ass of
itself, went on to the next business on the
paper. The incident was satisfactory, insofar
that it silenced the “ respectable ” brigade who
have found a pleasure in supporting the fiction
that no man dare impugn the honor of Parliament
at its own awful bar. Parliament, in this case,
was compelled to swallow a mild pill in the form
of a suggestion that “scoundrels and blacklegs”
are often returned at the top of the poll. Un-
happily, there was not sufficient grit in the
cushion-puncher to achieve a bolder result. He
might have localised the Jabez Balfour case,
and given the “ blackleg” term a more forcible
application. The Victorian Assembly recog-
nised this glorious possibility by letting the
gentleman depart in peace. As men of the
world, the members were entitled to argue that
lying is inseparable from modern politics, whilst
personal profligacy is not incompatible with
public honesty ox even with sturdy patriotism.
They couldn’t find a political defence for finan-
cial impurity in the abstract, or deny its exist-
ence within the sacred walls of the ’Ouse. The
one great sin of a soiled Senate has found it
out, and the huge, vague parson may continue
to work that fact for all he is worth. Privi-
leged abuses breed privileged abuse.

People’s Banks.
Mr. B. R. Wise, of Sydney, recently struck a
new remedy for the ever-present depression,
said remedy being the establishment of
“ People’s Banks,” after the fashion of those
now existing in Germany and Italy. The
People’s Bank, as it exists in those countries,
is a purely co-operative affair which lends
money to the agricultural classes at the
minimum rate of interest, and mainly on per-
sonal security. Each bank works within a
certain narrow radius. Inside its own little
circle it knows everybody, and estimates its
security by that personal knowledge. The
amounts lent are mostly small, but among a
poor population, and in a land where the cost
of living is very low, it is an inestimable benefit,
and its losses are so limited as to be
practically nil. The experiment of lending
money to the poorest class of the population,
on personal security, and at lowest current rate
U interest —the same rate which is commonly
charged on gilt-edged securities—looks reckless
inough, but after a trial of many years its
mccess has been sufficiently demonstrated.
Therefore, Mr. B. R. Wise, who is essentially a
man of book-knowledge and slow to learn by
personal observation, wants to establish
People’s Banks all over Australia, and produce
the same blessed result. He reasons that as
these institutions have succeeded in Continental
Europe they must succeed here, just as he
reasons that because Freetrade exists in England
it ought to exist here. But to any man except
the slavish copyist, it is sufficiently obvious
that the People’s Bank, if transplanted to Aus-
tralia, would be a ghastly and utter failure.
All the circumstances which have made it a
success elsewhere are wanting. The first
requirement is a stationary population—a popu-
lation which has fixed itself in its hereditary
village for centuries, so that the bank can
lend not only on the personal character of
its client, but on the character of his father
and his grandfather, and all the rest of his
family, and so that it can feel quite sure of
finding its debtor in the same old thatched
cottage when it wants him, even should it be
20 years hence or more. And the next essential
is a penurious population of the kind which
saves up pennies and half-pennies, and invests
its microscopic savings carefully, so as to
furnish the capital with which to run
the People’s Bank. But, in Australia,
with its migratory population, most of
which never had any available grandfather and
never stayed long enough in one place to estab-
lish a character which any bank would lend
money upon, and with its tendency to put its
small savings on the Melbourne Cup, and its
whole-souled contempt for the loan of 50s. which
is the staple transaction of the People’s Bank,
and its utter want of all the plodding charac-
teristics of the German or Italian peasant, the
experiment is hopeless. The People’s Banks
were built to suit a particular kind of humanity,
and they will suit no other variety. They
grow in their own especial soil, and can’t bear
transplantation.

,§.A. Register (Aug. 16), in a leading article
comments on the decision of the Ministry to hang
Brown, the Lovely Gully murderer. The man
Page, referred to, who shot his sweetheart at
Port Lincoln last year, was defended by Premier
Kingston, Q.C., and sentenced to death, but
spared by the Executive :
It is incomprehensible, except upon a sadly uncom-
plimentary hypothesis, how practically the same Ministers
could pardon Page and yet show no clemency to Brown.
But we welcome their latest action as a sign of their in-
tention to be just now if they never were before. We
welcome it the more particularly because if they had
dared many times more to outrage the public sense of
oght, as they did so shamelessly in the case of the Port
Lincoln murderer, they would have been the means of in-
troducing Lynch law into South Australia.
Ihe Register, then, can see no difference
between a murder madly committed under the
influence of jealousy or disappointed love, and
a murder done in apparent cold-blood, as in the
case of Brown, for the sake of gain 1 As to
the Register’s suggestion of Lynch law it may
>e here remarked that the public when in its
sober senses, in most cases, does not desire blood
mt is usually quite satisfied with more or less
extreme punishment short of death. It is a
poor judgment which will not stand the test of
Slx months’ consideration, yet who will dare to
Sil y that were murderers kept alive in gaol six
months after conviction public sentiment would
“ ofc » * u most cases, overwhelmingly favour their
reprieve? Taking recent cases, Page would
mot, under such circumstances, be hanged, nor
would Jordan, the negro butchered the other
day. Nor would any man or woman be executed
whose crime was the outcome of passion or
drink, and not of mercenary calculation.
Nicholls and Lester, who assassinated poor
workmen for the sake of their clothes and
silver watches, would be hanged with enthusi-
asm even if they were kept in gaol 25 years ;
but Montgomery and Williams would not be
hanged if their case had now to be calmly con-
sidered. The Register allows its Bakerian
detestation of Premier Kingston to get the
better of its judgment.

The new rule, which fixes a very narrow time
limit to the speech of Maoriland politicians,
worked well in the budget debate. Out of 72
members no less than 54 had their say on
the subject, and among them they said about
every thing which could be said, and a good
many other things besides. Yet the debate
was finished in a week, instead of dragging
over a month as it would have certainly done
under the old system. Nobody is ever con-
verted by Parliamentary speeches ; when the
longestdebate on record is finished the vote is just
the same as if there had been no debate at all.
The principal objects of a long debate are to
advertise the bores of the House with a view to
next election ; to enable certain persons of the
kind who must talk or burst (there are such
persons, just as there are persons who must
play Juliet or burst) to get their undigested
eloquence off their chests; to give time for
lobbying and buying votes ; and to tire out the
other side and snatch a division when the
weakly members have collapsed through sheer
weariness and gone home. And as none of
these things do any good to the country, the
rule which condenses every voluble bore into
30 minutes is a blessed innovation. Even
Shiels might have been a man of point and
brevity if he had been smitten by such a rule
in his extreme youth.

Chief Justice Madden’s sermons are not to
be laughed at when there is an embezzler in
the case. The truth is beautiful and pure, not-
withstanding that it promptly gets lost in the
Smellbourne financial stink. Hear Dr. Madden
to a “respectable” criminal; “You are one
more of the extremely long line of regretable
cases which assert themselves with alarming
persistency, cases which are extremely
dangerous to the community. It has been
shown that in most of the good qualities of men
you are exemplary, and had succeeded in estab-
lishing yourself in the position of trust and
confidence with those with whom you were
associated, but, singular and paradoxical as it
may seem, that is almost the invariable
condition of the offender in these cases.
People do not get into a position of trust
until they have established confidence in other
people, and these cases show that there is no
security to the community from the instinctive
honor of men who have been honorable up to
that time. On perhaps strong provocation they
have abandoned the principles of a lifetime,
and this breaks up all trust and confidence in
commercial affairs in commercial confidence,
which are inseparable from the career of a
successful community.” Dr. Madden, preaching
that confidence is broken up, and stating why it
broke, is a deal better than Premier Patterson
swearing that confidence was only a little sick,
and is now restored to health again. Unfor-
tunately, the waxed-moustachio’s sermons are
not addressed to the men who debauched the
embezzlers with their dangerous example of
“ fraud without punishment.”
Robert Stout, the teetotal fanatic, of Maori-
land, still gets up at intervals to declare wail-
fully that Ballance, when on his death-bed,
bequeathed him the leadership of the Demo-
cratic party, and that Seddon brutally and
aggressively jumped the billet. The Bulletin
doesn’t like Seddon any too much, but it likes
him better than Stout, for the reason that
Stout is a K.C.M.G. already, while Seddon is
only thinking of becoming one. Moreover, it
objects to Stout because he was Vogel’s
accomplice, because of his attempts to break up
the Democratic party, because of his awful
land-grant railway, and still more because
he publicly wept tears of joy over his
bauble, when he heard that he had fallen
into a knighthood. Also his dismal sobbing
over his alleged claims to the Premiership is
very wearisome, and his story is too thin to be
believed by any grown up person of reasonable
intelligence. The idea that Ballance, who was
about the brainiest Democrat in Australasia,
should have professed to give away his billet to
a successor appointed by himself, as if it was a
piece of private property, is absurd ; and the
idea that Stout, lawyer and KjO.M.G., should,
of all men, have been that successor, is equally
absurd. There is a soft and unreliable streak
in Robert Stout which unfits him for leader-
ship, and Ballance knew it as well as any-
body.

The June elections to the Parliament of Ontario,
the core of the Canadian Confederacy, were the
most remarkable ever held in the province. In
the Dominion Parliament the Conservatives are
in power as high-tariff Protectionists. In
Ontario, the Liberal party, composed of mode-
rate Protectionists with a good sprinkling of
Freetraders, has always been supreme. The
recent elections only settled them more firmly
in power. But a new political factor has arisen
in the shape of the Patrons of Industry, an
association of farmers disgusted with Protec-
tion, and advocating reform in all directions.
These have only been organising for two or
three years, yet they elected 16 members in a
House of 94 ; the Liberals being credited with
49, and the Conservatives with 27. To Ontario
and Canada, this irruption of free-trading
radicals is even more startling than the onset
of Labor into the New South Wales Parliament
seemed to the old parties in 1891. The Patrons
came in on the sole ground of reform ; the
strife between Liberals and Conservatives was
dominated by t he sectarian issue. Thanks to
the opposition between French Catholics and
English Protestants, nourished by the separate-
school system, religious animosity rages in
Canada with extraordinary virulence. The
Ontario Conservatives joined hands with the
Protestant Protective Association, which reaches
the acme of bigotry in proposing to endow
Protestantism with public funds and disendow
Catholicism altogether. The result was that
Ontario Catholics wei’e thrown into the arms of
the Liberals and contributed largely to the
Liberal victory.
The pan-colonial conference in Canada has
given a pleasant picnic, at tax-payers’ expense,
to a score of pleasant gentlemen; and it has
done nothing else. The object of the Canadian
promoters was to boom preferential trade
relations within the British empire, in the hope
that at next general elections Canadian tariff
reformers—steadily growing in numbers and
importance, and defrauded of their best
and natural trade with the U.S.—might be
persuaded to take the shadow instead of the
substance. When England—which cannot give
trade-preferences to Australia or Canada or
the Cape without embroiling herself in Europe
—sent Lord J ersey as a mere spectator, she
uttered the doom of the conference. When
Queensland, New South Wales, and Maoriland,
through their delegates, opposed the proposi-
tions for preferential trade they executed the
sentence. Nobody anticipated any better
result. In Canada itself the conference was
chiefly noticed to be scoffed at; and the
Farmers’ Sun, the organ of Ontario democrats,
remarked that the projected deep canal from
Montreal to Toronto was of infinitely greater
importance than its “visionary proposals.” All
that remains is to foot the bills of Suttor,
Thynne, Fraser, Smith and Co., and yearn
for the time when Australia will be governed
by men with sense and principle enough not to
waste the people’s money wantonly.
A controversy about the sinfulness of cards,
dancing, and theatre-going has, for some weeks
past, been raging furiously in Sydney Tele-
graph. A lot of interesting correspondents
have howled dismally on various aspects of the
subject, about the most interesting of the crowd
being “ A Christian Mother,” who demands, in
a .terrible tone of voice: “Can we imagine
Peter, James, and John, with their Divine
Lord, sitting down to a game of w'hist, or Paul
dancing ?” Obviously no one can imagine such
a thing, and, therefore, the “Christian
Mother” retires triumphant, and the sinfulness
of theatres, dancing, and card-playing is fully
demonstrated. But when one seriously con-
siders the matter, the evidence doesn’t look
quite so conclusive. No one can seriously
imagine one of the aforesaid Apostles wearing
a black tail coat and a stove-pipe hat,
for instance, or writing to the Daily
Telegraph, or having his hair brushed by
steam - power, or travelling in a tram or
a steamer, or getting a Turkish bath, or
being vaccinated, or keeping a dog, or eating
barracoota, or doing several billion other
things. The Apostle, as he is viewed through
the haze of 19 centuries, seems to have been
a person who lived nohow in particular,
had no visible means of support, was
always tramping excitedly from place to place,
had no fixed church or habitation, was dressed
principally in a long, high-coloured wrap, and
lived in a constant state of hysterical exalta-
tion. The modern “ Christian Mother” is so
fond of picturing her apostle in this misty and
frantic condition that she can’t even realise the
poor man as having had his pants mended in
an ordinary every-day fashion; and when the
sinfulness of any modern amusement is dis-
cussed she always points out that some apostle
or other wouldn’t have done it, and thereby
settles the question at once. The real fact
being that nobody, at this distance of time, can
seriously realise that an apostle ever did any-
thing except preach and work miracles, so that,
according to the “Christian Mother” kind of
logic, these are the only legitimate occupations
left to us.

Dear Bulletin, —I contend that the Q. Sugar
Acl, equitably administered, will make the
fortunes of hundreds of struggling farmers. Is
it equitably administered ? Let the history of
the circs, under which the Kelly’s Camp Co.
was prevented from getting the Government
guarantee supply the answer. The Kelly’s
Camp crowd (Isis Scrub, Bundaberg district)
formed a company ; and the Govt, employed
one A. B. Jones to value the land. Jones
is a man of long experience in the work, and
claims that he gave an appraisement something
below the full value. Cran and Co. own a mill
in the near vicinity, and they object to a co-
operative mill because it is competitive, and
possibly also because it will determine by results
the true value of the cane. At present Cran
and Co. give 3s. to 6s. a ton. The Mackay
Co-op. mills give 12s. 6d. to 14s. Density
accounts for a good deal of difference. Do you
see now where the Cran shoe pinches? There-
fore, somebody (was it the Grans?) got up a dis-
cussion in the papers about the right of Govt,
to lend money at 5 per cent, to enable co-op.
mills to compete with capitalist mills built
with money costing 8 per cent. To block the
‘Kelly’s Camp crowd somebody (was it Cran?)
took a trip to Brisbane and had an inter-
view with Minister Philp. Evidently somebody
(? a Cran) told Philp the land had been over-
valued by Jones, and got Govt, to get it re-
valued by somebody else—who happens to have
been formerly connected with the sugar busi-
ness. The new valuation was less than half
Jones’, and Philp has refused the guarantee,
telling a recent deputation that “ he had been
assured (by whom ?) that there was sufficient
mill accommodation already.” So things stand.
Between the upper millstone of bank monopoly
and the nether one of private monopoly indus-
try in this province is ground mighty small
nowadays. And it deserves it. The asses who
went and voted for “ strong man ” MTlwraith
have only themselves to thank for their kicking.
—Yours, VVurry M.
Instead of the usual annual increases, New
South Wales public-school returns, just pub-
lished, show a falling-off for the year, as com-
pared with the preceding 12 months, both in
the average attendance and enrolment of
scholars between the ages of six and fourteen
years inclusive. Also, during the latter half-
year of the returns many more pupils failed to
complete the regulation minimum attendance
than during the first half-year, which period,
compared again with the preceding six months,
also shows an increased non-attendance—“ the
depression,” of course, being solely responsible
for the foregoing. To check the increasing
tendency of parents to take their children from
school, and put them to w’ork, before attaining
the prescribed age, the Education authorities
have found it necessary to raise the standard of
the examination required to be passed by pupils
who wish to leave school before reaching the
age of 14. The examination is so stiff now that
scholars who have passed their junior exam,
have lately been known to fail to negotiate this
examination. The check is not altogether an
unnecessary one, for no child should, if possible,
be allowed to be sent out into the world under
the age of 14.

A slab of Foreign-trade wisdom from Melb.
Argus :
And to whom are the men to sell the produce they raise
from the land ? They must sell abroad. And what will
these men obtain for their produce ? They will obtain
commodities. Their customers abroad can only pay in
commodities for the produce. Surely the customers who
buy our produce should be allowed to send as much in
return in commodities as possible. The High Tariff oartv
say “ No.” r
It is about time someone contradicted this old
wheeze about “commodities.” Australia has
exported in 30 years about .£300,000,000 of
gold, in order to pay people who want cash
instead of commodities. Britain has exported,
in 30 years, £380,000,000 in gold, and imported
£447,000,000; Francehasimported £403,000,000
and exported £276,000,000 ; and other countries
have done, and are constantly doing, likewise,
for the same reason. It is estimated that there
are in the whole world about £1,235,000,000 in
gold coin and bullion, and about the same amount
in silver, and this stupendous mass of mineral is
constantly being humped round the earth, and
shipped in great boxes to the furthest ends of the
globe to pay for produce purchased which the
seller won’t take out in commodities. More-
over, the Argus logic bursts up right on its own
doorstep. Victoria in ’92 exported nearly
£1,000,000 worth to France, and got back
£60,000 worth, and the theory that payment is
made in commodities is thus 94 per cent. out.
In the same year it imported from Canada to
the value of £42,000, and sent back absolutely
nothing except cash; therefore, in this instance,
the ancient Freetrade wheeze is just 100 per
cent, bunkum. The simple fact is that every
country buys and sells where it can, and is con-
stantly importing and exporting great masses of
gold and silver in payment, and the commodity
idea is only a figure of speech, and not much of
one at that.

“Lady Cook” seems to have raised some
curiosity in dull old-world minds, and Aus-
tralian editors have rushed to quote her views,
by propounding the conundrum—“ Should the
Poor Marry ?” Why “ the poor ” only should
be selected doesn’t seem to strike anybody as
worth questioning. Should the poor sleep ?
Should the poor eat ? What has marriage —as
marriage—as a purely sexual coupling—to do
with poverty ? Should birds mate ? Are “ the
poor ” different in sexual functions to “ the
rich?” Parody Shylock’s complaint and apply
it to this subject. “Should the poor marry ?”
is about the most senseless question propounded
during this Jin de siecle. Should anybody
marry ? And Lady Cook declares this law
If they have small incomes and extravagant habits or
tastes, or if they are deficient in energy and business
aptitudes, or even in moral principles, then they had far
better remain single.
Very well, then. If they do remain technically
single, what then? This needs plain talk—
unvarnished, realistic thought. Has marriage
—as marriage, and not as a branch of commerce
or religion—anything whatever to do with
incomes, or extravagance, or deficiency in busi-
ness aptitude and energy, or loose morals ?
Bluntly : Marriage is the civilised symbol of
the universal instinct of the procreation of the
species. Rob the poor, or any other class, of
marriage, and you foster !
Max O’Rell gives a foretaste of what is going
to happen to Australia in his forthcoming book.
In a French magazine he says that the lord
and master of Australia is the working-man,
who will neither profit by his opportunities nor
let others profit. The Australian working-
man is lazy, drunken, a sieve—thinking only
of himself, and nothing of his country. He
idles in the towns, while the land cries for
labour. Finally, he ought to imitate the China-
jpan whom he despises. Of course, this is only
a bare extract ; but if it fairly represents the
article, and if the article fairly represents the
book, Australia can only add Max O’Rell to
the innumerable company who have enjoyed her
freedom, and her sunshine, and her hospitality*
and then gone away bo libel her. Max O’Rell
saw only a corner of Australia, and even of
that corner it is impossible to generalise with-
out many drawbacks and reservations. As his
words now stand they are in the main untrue.
On the whole, the Australian working-man is
neither lazy nor drunken ; he is a sieve—if that
means careless and improvident; he does stay
in the towns and pray to Government for
work, while the country goes untilled ; the
Chinaman can teach him something in
the way of patient industry. But, never-
theless, Max O’Rell’s language gives a
false impression. With all his faults, the Aus-
tralian working-man is as good a specimen as
there is going. We would not change him for
the French or English peasant-slave or factory-
drudge—nor the best of him even for the ener-
getic American. For in morality and intelli-
gence he stands as high as any others of his
class, and higher than most. He has large
virtues to compensate small vices. If thrift-
less, he is generous ; if deficient in energy and
initiative for himself, he has zeal and sympathy
for others. He degenerates in the cities ; but
you must average him to know him. See him
in the bush, see him on the mines. The
Bulletin would pit the first ten thousand met
around Coolgardie against any workers in the
world—not as beasts of burden, not as money-
makers, not as saints, but as men.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Bank of Victoria shows a profit for the
past half-year of £29,921, and spends £28,339 in paying a
o per cent. div. The balance, which amounts to £1582,
goes to strengthen the position of the bank. Considering
all the possibilities that lie before a bank in these times,
this “strengthening” process is a ghastly joke. If the
Victoria would be content to pay 5 per oent. on its pre-
ference shares and reduce the div. on ordinary shares to
2J, it would be able to add about £20,000 per annum to
its reserve, which is little enough in a province where
“securities" have gone down into an abyss of deprecia-
tion, and no bottom is yet visible. And if it would pass
the div. on ordinary shares altogether, and carry £40,000
a year to reserve for the next three years, it would be
none too much, considering all the chances in the way of
depreciation which are yet ahead. The Bank of Victoria
has splendid possibilities, which are being steadily fooled
away in the frantic desire to pay divs. Meanwhile, the
£lO preference shares are selling at about £6, and the
ordinary shares have long since practically vanished out
of the exchange quotations, so the 5 per cent, dividend
hasn’t even the humble virtue of inspiring confidence.
• • • *
The Bank of Victoria, like all the rest, has been
seized with the mania for huge cash balances. Before re-
construction it kept about 1J millions on hand in a more
or less liquid form ; now that its depositors have been so
securely fixed that it can’t be rushed under any possible
circumstances, it keeps a cash balance of nearly 1J
millions. At least half this amount ought to be used at
once to pay off reconstructed depositors, and if the
depositors choose to renew at a lower rate of interest, as
many of them probably would, so much the better for the
institution, Just now almost every bank is remorselessly
squeezing gord customers, and "selling them up, and
wrecking good business, in order to swell its cash on
hand, and to show how securely the stable door has been
locked and barricaded after the horse is gone. The chief
business of a bank director these times seems to be to
gaze, with an aspect of fatuous joy, at an inflated money-
bag in the cellar, and report half-yearly on the increased
bulging thereof.
• • • •
Now that the awful burden of the 4$ per cent,
interest on reconswlndled deposits is beginning to be felt
in real earnest, many banking potentates would gladly sand-
bag or otherwise injure the once great Gyles Turner,
of the Commercial of Australia. That magnate decided
that it was neceswy to offer 4J per cent., and so great
was his reputation that every other bank, save the
E.S. and A.U., followed his example without question.
The solitary exception reconstructed mainly on a 4 per
cent, basis. To a hank with, say, £4,000,000 of deposits,
thatjp r cent, makes a difference of just £20,000 per
annum, which, in the case of an institution with a capital
of £500,000, means a dividend of 2J per cent, gratuitously
fooled away.

Matthias Larkin, the saint of the South Melh.
Building Society, was tried last week for another big
embezzlement of £3399, and sentenced to five years’ gaol.
This term, added to the six years he is already'serving,
makes eleven. Matthias was also tried on two more
chargi s, and was found not guilty; hut when the prose-
cution gets fully rested it will probably start afresh on a
few more oases, so that Mr. Larkin has great possibilities
before him. In Ins own humble way, the ex-hoss of the
South Melb. Building Society is now the most useful man
in Victoria. Whenever the public has doubts about the
purity of Victorian justice, and is indignant at the immu-
nity enjoyed by a few dozen conspicuous boom criminals,
the law rises un in its might and starts another prosecu-
tion against Mat. Lai kin, and to all appearance it will
continue doing so as long as that eminent saint lasts.
M itt.hias’ eleven years are not by any means too much
tor his frauds, hut if there are only eleven years' gaol
going it ought to be more equally divided.
Tn passing sentence on Brother Larkin, Judge
Midden informed that brassy apostle that ‘‘in the evil
history of an evil time his conduct stood out as a monu-
ment of woe to multitudes of people. He had been re-
sponsible for bringing down a storehouse of the savingsof
the people, and neither helplessness nor poverty had
stayed his depredating hand.” All of which is doubtless
true, hut so many other and bigger storehouses came
down, and so many other persons took the coin with a
depredating hand, and then went away on depredating
feet and were never troubled by any inquiries, that it
se' ins a pity Mat. Larkin should have all the eloquence to
himself.

When it transpired in the Victorian bankruptcy
court that ex-Speaker Davies had only £BO, and the
white flower of a blameless life wherewith to satisfy
liabilities of £281,000, Judge Molesworth, who evidently
regarded the aforesaid blossom as insufficient security,
decided to insist on the statutory payment of 7s. in the
£, and refused a certificate accordingly. But he thought-
fully pointed out that if the bankrupt felt aggrieved at
this decision, he could appeal to the Supreme Court to
have it revers-d. And Matthew D. does feel aggrieved,
and will probably take the hint, and will most likely get
his certificate in due course. Meanwhile, the general
opinion, in the highest land-boom circles, is that Judge
Molesworth is a low character,
• • • •
Melb. Argus, in the course of a bitter attack
on the proposed State Bank, remarks: “In May, last
year, the Vic. bank-note system was exposed to a hurri-
cane trial, and it emerged without injury from a strain
under which the Canadian and American systems would
assuredly have broken down.” Considering that the bank-
note system “ emerged ” simply because the banks
annexed the depositors’ money and paid the note-holders
with it, the above is cast-iron front in its best state of
development.
* « • «
It is only within the past six months that
certain Southern banks have “tumbled” to the fact that
the bill-clerk is one of the most important factors in the
banking community. As bankers now rely on di-count
accounts for a profit, a customer who cannot secure an
overdraft on ordinary securities can get any limit on dis-
count overdraft. A few cute traders having “dropped"
to the idea have got “discounting overdrafts” to amounts
ridiculously out of proportion to their capital, and it is
only now that two banks have ascertained that numbers
of their customers have regularly discounted the paper of
hopelessly insolvent customers month after month and
have thus got overdrafts for from five to ten times the
amount that either their stock, hook-debts, or property
warranted. The bill-clerks recently appointed by the
most heavily-struck banks, in this respect, are getting
salaries equal to the sub-managers.
• • • *
When one official of a big Australian concern
unlawfully annexes a hundred or two and gets a long
stretch of imprisonment his case is fully set forth in
the daily papers. Another official captures £7500, and
not a word is said in the press. The directors are dumb
as to the reason of that official having suddenly dis-
appeared. The smaller embezzler’s peculation is made
public, apparently, to let shareholders believe that th e
directorate is wide-awake, but the greater tinner’s is kept
dark—not (as many people suppose) to prevent a fall In
shares, but to enable those In the know to buy in, it
being now well-esta' lished that at least £lO,OOO that
should have been available for dividends has been mis-
appropriated during the past 16 months.

Another Melb. solicitor has had the atrociously
cruel sentence of one year’s suspension passed upon him
by a brutal Judge for getting away with a client’s cash.
The shark’s guileless plea was that he had not received
the money as a solicitor, but as an agent. A very foolish
plea indeed, in the light of judicial custom in such casts.
Supposing the court had taken him at his word and
elected to punish him as an agent and not as a solicitor,
why he might have got 12 months' quod 1 The wise man
nowadays who wants to lift another man’s property will
always do it in his capacity as an attorney.
• « • •
In Melb. Insolvency Court, the other day,
Judge Molesworth ordered certain parties to pay a dis-
puted amount of £137 into an insolvent estate, and
furthermore ordered those parties to pay the trustee's
costs in the action. The trustee’s solicitor made out a
little bill for £239, being costs incurred by the victor in
the struggle over £137, subject, of course, to more or less
reduction by the usual taxing officer. Fearful lest the
taxing officer should not reduce the bill verv considerably
the parties aforesaid applied to Judge Molesworth for
relief. They begged him to fix a fair amount of costs to
be paid along with the £137. His Honor fixed them at
£3l 10s., or less than one-tenth of the amount charged by
the trustee’s solicitor. Question —Will the unhappy
estate be victimised for the difference between £3l 10s.
and t’239, or will the Law which protected the individuals
come to the rescue of the estate ? Does anybody ever
come to the rescue of an insolvent estate?

Matthew Davies’chief creditor, apart from the
innumerable companies which were so good to him in his
golden prime, appears to be his late private secretary.
Yet_ the secretary didn't oppose the application for a
certif. He loved his former master too dearly, perhaps.
Doosid affecting situation—the secretary standing with a
sponge in readiness to wipe thousands off the slate for
the sake of auld lang syne I Suggestive of Dan’l Jaikes
kneeling to Wilfrid Denver in “ The Silver King.” “O
M ister Matthew, Master Matthew, I earned it all in your
service, and if you’ve lost it all in unfortunate specula-
tions you are the man to he pitied. What does money
matter to me, dear Master Matthew !” A financial melo-
drama might be written around that one pathetic scene.

loans will turn out equally disastrous in the end.

About a year ago a certain wild youth in-
herited a pot of money, and was promptly sued on bills
for several hundreds endorsed by a man who left
Australia just after the bills were discounted. The
lender was nonsuited, on the defence set up by the maker
that his signature was a forgery ; he, moreover, denied
all knowledge of the bills or their proceeds. The alleged
forger and absconder(who had been currently regarded as
a fugitive from justice) turned up again the other day,
and the first man he called on was the lender, to learn “ if
those bills had been met all right?” Then it came out
that the endorser had only gone to England to receive a
handsome legacy, and when he produced the youth’s
own receipt for the proceeds of the hills, the maker had
to part handsomely to square matters up.

• » • •
Developments at the Central (8.H.) to which
are due the resumption of smelting and consequent rise
in shares, would, four years ago, have sent the exchanges
wild and caused a rise of more pounds than shares have
now risen shillings. The developments at and below the
600 ft. level are the most valuable of the year at Broken
Hill.
The long-standing feud between the Melb. and
S.A. directors of the Central will probably continue until
the office, and with it the practical boss-ship of the
mine, is removed to Adelaide. At present, the
Adelaide directors, being in a minority, may only fume
and make recommendations, which the Melb. crowd
ignore. The maintenance of the divided board is profit-
able to the directors, but decidedly expensive to the
shareholders.
Melb. directors of the Central have been
credited with a desire to maintain the system of disposing
of the mine’s output to the Dry Creek Smelting Co.
Chaffey Baker, Adelaide director and chief advocate of
local smelting, discussing the subject with a Broken Hill
interviewer, said that the Melb. board preferred managing
the office in Queen-street to managing the mine. Whereat
more trouble between the two directorates.
Proposals to re-open B.H. South mine are based
on the disposal abroad at alleged satisfactory rates of the
whole of the sulphide concentrates produced by the Han-
cock jigs at the mine before the stoppage. The erection
of another jig and economies in working are proposed
which will, it is said, reduce the cost of concentration by
ss. per ton. There is now a permanent market for the
concentrates which, unfortunately, the South people have
not yet shown they are able to produce at or below the
price obtained for them. Wherefore, despite the promised
economies the prospects from re-starting are not so
enticing as they might be. It is certain that operations,
prior to the stoppage, resulted in a slight loss for every
ton of ore concentrated.
Proprietary assisfcant-general-manager Stewart
from Spain, who arrived in Adelaide early this month
and was met there by gen.-manager Howell, went on a
trip to Tasmania before starting work at Broken Hill. It
is alleged that Howell, who had no voice in the selection
of the new man, who will practically be manager of the
Proprietary, looks at him in advance with a cold eye.
The ranch-lauded Junction North has fallen on
evil days, and the sudden depletion of its rich sandstone,
coincident with the silver fall, rendered leaching opera-
tions unprofitable. Recently a contract was arranged for
the mining and treatment of sulphide, of which the mine
possesses a fair quantity, on royalty, but the operations
were a failure, as at least several people can depose. The
mine is now raising what rich sandstone it can find—
which is little—and hopes to sell it at a profit.
* * • ♦
_ Mount Drysdale (N.S.W.) mine expects this
time to get over 1600 oz. from 130 tons stone—all raised
within eight days.
* • » *
Many “wonderful developments” from Wya-
long, and no crushings save 10-ton driblets. And where
are the other batteries that were to go up? Isn’t there
enough stone ?
One correspondent says Wyalong hasn’t pro-
duced 2000 oz. gold in all—about equal to the monthly
yield of each of several Far Northern claims. There are
no authoritative figures; but even putting the total at
40000 z., it’s not a brilliant return for, say, six months’
work for, say, 1000 men.
Things at Wyalong are just this way : there’s a
good deal of gold in rich mnllocky leaders which may
average 6in. all round. Country soft, and no explosives
needed. But there are no properly defined reefs in Wya-
long proper, and what will become of those leaders when
they reach solid rock, any miner can guess.
But, supposing they do last, and pay to work,
it is plain the gold can’t be worked profitably from a
hundred pot-hole shafts on as many prospecting areas—
where you’ve no sooner started to drive than you’re up
to the other fellow’s boundary. It doesn’t pay to put up
machinery to work that sort of reef at a depth.' Now I hat
the surface stuff has been pretty well tumbled over, it’s
time the owners of a dozen claims consolidated into a
single lease, and started to work systematically—if they
have faith in the country. “ If!”

London Economist remarks that from Feb.,
*B5, to Jan., ’BB, 28 companies with an aggregate capital
close on £5,000,000, were floated in England for working
gold-properties in Queensland ; out of this, well over
£4,000,000 went to the promoters and vendors! . . .
Westralia, the Economist hints, is going the same way as
Queensland. ‘‘Of the two companies announced this
week, one proposes to pay away, to commence with,
£45,000 out of a nominal capital of £65,000-while the
other has contracted to pay the promoters ami vendors
£40,000 out of £50,000 ! ”
* * * *
The time will soon come when suspicion will be
against all proposed mining companies that do not offer
preferential divs. to the investing public. Floaters of
mines should at least say: “We offer investors a non-
cumulative but preferential div. of 7 per cent, on their
capital. When the investors have been allotted this per-
centage, the vendors and promoters will get the same, if
it is there, and the surplus, if any, will be shared all
round.”
