• • • *
For Titanic impudence commend ns to the
“Pearce prospecting party” crowd. The man who
wants to float a prospect is one thing, but the man
who wants to float the prospect of a prospect is a
multitude in himself. One Pearce, of Westralia (not
John Pearce, who lately managed the N.S.W. Golden
Gully), wants camels to go prospecting. Certain bene-
factors of their race propose to float Pearce and his
camels into a No Liability Co., in which they will retain
110 fuily-paid-up shares, 500 contributing shares (£5 each)
being offered to the innocent public. The said pro-
moter-benefactors are named in an Arous advt., and
to their names is added “and others.” “And
others ” is delicious. There is a non-issue of 390
shares! “These being held in reserve for future issue
will form a splendid reserve-fund, as they will no doubt
greatly increase in value as discoveries are made.” Why
did they not make the non-issue 390,000,000 shares’
The splendid reserve would then have been a million
times splendider. But, after all. why should the public
contribute £2500 to share possible divs. with a parcel of
promoters who apparently risk nothing but their reputa-
tions in the concern ?
In striking contrast to the “ Pearce Co.” is the
party proposed to be equipped and officered from Ballarat.
The prospectors will receive expenses and no salary—
their discoveries will be remunerated by shares in the 00.
which has franked them. There are no shares issued as
paid-up, or other monstrosities. The capital is to be
£l2OO in 2400 shares of 10s. each to lie fully subscribed.
Prof, Dawson, of Ballarat School of Mines, is on the pro-
visional board. The leader of the prospectors, Mr,
Muller, himself subscribes for 50 shares, 25 of which he
leaves for 12 months in the hands of the co. The Ballarat
scheme is a mo-t reasonable one.
• * * *
One Rail, at Ballarat (Vic.), called a meeting
the other day to establish a prospecting association, and
his scheme bids well for success. £5O per week is to be
raised from 1000 contributing shares at Is. each, and the
large unexplored areas round the original Golden City
are to be systematically examined. A big improvement
on the Govt, prospecting-vote system.

* • * •
When the City of Glasgow Bank made its his-
toric burst in the latter Seventies, one of the items which
came under the astonished eye of the liquidator was an
advance of £90,000 to an alleged “ dairy farmer.” The
official went out to view this copious borrower, and to
have a look at the security, and estimate the chances of
repayment. The borrower proved to be a phenomenally
illiterate person, in a small way of business, and stone-
deaf. His dairy farm consisted of four acres of leased
land, and his whole stock was embodied in four cows, all
well stricken in years, a flea-bitten grey horse, also in the
sere and miserable leaf, an old cart, and two dilapidated
milk-cans. He knew nothing about the bank, or the
£90,000, or anything else. But at last, after much con-
sideration, he vaguely recollected that, to oblige a certain
kirk elder of his acquaintance, he had laboriously fixed
his signature to something—he had no idea what—and
had received £lO for doing it. That was all that was ever
discovered ; who grabbed the £90,000 is a mystery even
to this day. When some happy family of directors writes
off anything between £lO,OOO and half-a-million as
“ losses,” and the shareholders, as usual, get no reliable
particulars, it always roundaboutly recalls this old story
to The Bulletin’s mind.

The collapse of the Maoriland kauri-gum trade
is attributed to the American importers being unable to
shift their heavy stocks. There was quite a boom in gura
during the erection of Chicago Exhibition, through the
enormous quantities of varnish used on the buildings.
Nine-tenths of the world’s supply of "copal" varnish is
made from M.L. gum, and it is said that a £lO per ton
export duty would speedily give to the Maorilanders a
monopoly of the manufacture of this commodity.

846 Macquarle-street North, Sydney,
August 28, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The secretary of the Sydney Women’s Literary
Club rose, the other day, in one of the various
hired chambers the club has been assembling in
for some time past and triumphantly announced
a surplus ot .£2B. And on the dignity of that
surplus it was at once suggested that the club
should burst forth into all the old glories of a
room of its own —to reduce the surplus as
quickly as possible, I suppose.
I hope, if they do again achieve their aim, thatan
effort will be made to place things on a more
artistic footing. Why not run a sort of Austral
Salon? Why not be eulchawed, body and soul,
while you’re at it ? A few yards of art silk—some
impressionist blobs on the door panels—a fair
notion of posing on the part of the committee —a
soulful name, “The Lotus Ladies,” for example,
coupled with an everlasting tea-kettle, and the
shabby, little-known Women’s Literary Club
would blossom forth as a feature of Sydney
society, I’ll venture to bet. And perhaps its light
and airy influence might even provoke the dismal
suffrage ladies into buying red umbrellas ! Aus-
tralia is a land where we want plenty of music
and red umbrellas.
The cyclamen show (that was what it really was»
though they called it a daffodil and camellia), held
by N.S.W. Horticultural Society in that munici-
pal sarcophagus—by day, at least—Sydney Town
Hall, was set a-moving on 22nd ult. by Lady
Duff (multiplied by the Duff girls and Oapt.
Tedding) whose way of doing a show distinctly
differs from any other person’s way. Ordinary
people go to look at one another more than the
flowers, but Guvment House tends strictly to
business and does the whole thing in a visibly
laborious spirit, from the honey-pots all of a row
to that eternal exhibit of potted ferns which we
have always with us. The present vice-regal
party doing their rigid duty by a flower-show
always reminds me in a hazy sort of way of people
trying to balance along a kerbstone —they looked
as if something dreadful would happen if they
glared to right or left of the exhibits.
Perhaps the most original things in the show
were the bouquets in the water (bottled bouquets,
I call them), a sort of floral pickling to keep
flowers fresh for a reasonably long time. A
“new and rare flower ” —staged in the vicinity of
a specimen of the baggy-mouthed orchid Mr.
Joseph Chamberlain has made so eternally famous
at ’Otne—was more like the birth of a shaving-
brush than anything idyllic, and I must protest
against the labelling an exhibit of our native
flowers with common school-boy nicknames. Aus-
tralian botanical knowledge must be only skin-
deep when one sees indigenous flora solemnly and
officially tagged with such names as “Bread and
Beef,” “ Sally,” and so on. There mayn’t be
®uch in a name, but if ever our flowers become
the fashion—as they are sure to do when flower-
shows have done their fell work and made them a
rarity—then the sooiety reporter, describing the
bride’s bouquet, will have a struggle to come out
of the ordeal poetically. Imagine it! “ "he beau-
tiful bouquet of long trails of Sally in a romantic
setting of bread-and-beef contrasted with a fringe
of hurdy-gurdies and old man.”
The first prize for decorated tables went to a
mild and harmless commingling of daffodils a,nd
violets and greenery that was an ideal decoration
for a school-girl’s birthday-tea, but lacking in
design and originality. Evidently the amateurs
who wrestle with these competitions are not bold
in the pursuit of color-contrasts. The new dining-
table with a revolving centre (shown at the Agri-
cultural Show) was displayed in all the pomp and
circumstance of a rough-dried table-cloth —but I
always notice the same defect about Sydney
flower-shows. The staging looks as if it had
arranged itself, although any decoratively-inclined
person can get miles of “ art ” rag and fussy paper
for I don’t know how little.
A concert in aid of St. John’s Church, Darling-
hurst, came off in the Y.M.C.A. hall the other
Wednesday with a certain amount of success, the
performers all being very nicely dressed, at any
rate. Admiraless Bowden-Smith and the two
daughters were present, and a fairly good audience,
as times go, turned up. This is the season of
charity dances —not charity concerts, which are
early, sandwichless festivities a little behind date
just now. Mrs. Herbert Lord, Miss 0. Delohery,
and Mr. Bayley Macarthur were the successful
soloists of the affair, Miss Delohery being the only
singer whose efforts were florally rewarded,
though I’m sure Mr. Macarthur deserved a but-
tonholer at least for his erotic rendering of “ Sun-
shine above.” The names of Mons. Kowalski and
Edgar ’Cello Strauss lent a classic hue to the rose-
pink programme, and the Orpheus Club was
down for three items. By the. way, if the aforesaid
club puts on many more frills it ought to be warned
off concert platforms for life. The clubbists won’t
sing last on any account, and simply make hay of
the sequence of the programme. But, after all, it
is really a confession of weakness to clamor for
the best positions on the bill. People who hold
their audiences are never afraid of empty benches.
From W. H. Paling and Co. I received “Pal-
ing’s Dance Annual,” an inviting-looking book of
favourite dance-music (including the always popu-
lar “Blue Danube” waltz), which anybody who
pipes to other people’s dancing ought to move
mountains to obtain. I have also received, more
in sorrow than in anger, the “ Lady Noeline ”
waltz dedicated to Mrs, Brough by G. Stayton;
and the “Sunny Side” waltz [not dedicated toTal-
mage), by E. S. Pitt. After carefully considering
the weak and pointless compositions aforesaid,
one is regretfully forced to the conclusion that
Strauss’ laurels are safe so far.
On the other hand, Hector Maclean (who, as the
local head and front of the Trinity Coll, exams, is
the author of all the musical mischief that has
befallen us) sends me a neat and well-written
ballade in E flat for the piano, and I was also
pleased to receive “The Aurora Australia” waltz
dedicated to Henri Kowalski by Miss Lilian
Morris. The “Aurora” is a cheeky little com-
position in the Kowalskian vein, and is very bright
to listen to in some parts. Talking of musical
successes reminds me that Maude Fitz-Stubbs’
reckless-sounding “Heather” waltz was played
by Dan. Godfrey’s band of the Grenadier Guards
at the Imperial Institute before the Royal Family
on the 14th July, when the only English com-
poser’s names on the embossed card were Sullivan,
Macfarren, and Fitz-Stubbs 1
Talking as I was, five inches ago, about flower-
shows, reminds me of the bazaar and vegetable set-
out held at an up-the-line N.S.W. town, the other
day. The wife of one of the district dignitaries
was squiring the Guvmentouse party among
the violets and carrots and things, and after
nearly knocking her front-teeth out over the pro-
nunciation of botanical tags came to signal grief
at last over a show of little apples. “ Are those
American apples?” queried one of the visitors.
“Oh, no, they’re New York pippins,” replied the
lady, airily passing on to exhibit onety-oneth.
The first of the Urso-Sapio concerts happened
on Monday evening at Sydney Centenary Hall in
the presence of a crowd fairly numerous, but
almost wildly enthusiastic. Most of the interest
centred in Madame Urso, who was here in ’Bo—-a
sufficiently recent back date, for even the toughest
spinster will own up bravely to being over 14. So
Camilla Urso got the clamorous reception due to
a favourite who re-visits her old audiences.
In a creamy costume ’of lace and satin (not so
fantastic that the artiste’s gew-gaws were louder
than her music, as was the case with Norman-
Neruda, who kept all the women wondering how
on earth she avoided getting her waving chiffon
sleeves caught in the catgut) and a sprinkling of
enviable diamonds, it was at once apparent that
Madame meant to efface herself in her music,
which was music, if you like. As a violiniste, she
is electric. She plays from memory, which always
makes each moroeau seem like an impromptu ; and
never once raised her eloquent eyes from her lov-
ingly-held fiddle. Double-barrelled encores chased
her soli—a “ suite ” composed in 1783 by a clead-
and-gone genius appropriately named Rust, and
the “Witches” dance (Paganini) which lengthened
her two items into five. Even to the non-expert,
Madame’s music was an unspeakably delicious
treat.
The handsome and pleasant Madame de Yere
Sapio, in blue satin sprigged over with gold and
trimmed with bands of jewelled gold braid, lifted
up her beautifully-produced soprano with most
effect in an operatic aria which she sang so easily
that the technical difficulties of the music were
never suggested. Madame’s voice sounds like the
vocal apotheosis of Francis Saville; there is the
same sweet, fine, bird-like quality, clear articulation
and brightness of production. Mind you, I said
“ apotheosis,” because Saville left Australia to get
the top polish that glistens all over Sapio’s sing-
ing. She was wildly encored, too, and so was her
husband, Signor Sapio, for bis own “Gavotte
Pompadour,” and Herr Scherek got a call
for a rather anxiously-played little thing of
Handel’s. By the way, Scherek ought to get his
hair cut in the interests of his own eyesight, for it
oasts quite a yew-tree-like shade on his music-
book. Madame Urso’s husband, the urbane M.
Lu&re, looked after the front of the house in several
languages.
Sydney Lancers turned out, as usual, to escort
the Governor at the opening of Parliament.
They being pure amateurs, and in nowise bound
to perform such services, it has been customary
for vioeregality to “refresh” them on such occa-
sions. Gov. Carington used to have champagne
opened for them, and the tactful Lord Jersey
followed suit, but Gov. Duff, at his first
“opening,” only opened beer; —“colonial.” This
didn’t suit the much - millinered_ brigade, so
this time they made up their minds in
advance not to accept beer ; when a couple
of gubernatorial flunkeys approached them
with partially-filled tankards of flat she-oak
the gallant Lanceahs—who are so particular that
they once actually sought to expel a man from
their ranks because they found out that he sold
socks for a living—spurned the base liquid and
said with one voice, “We Lanoeahs nevah dwink
bee-ah 1” The Duff menials sniggered incredu-
lously, but took back the tanglefoot—which will,
no doubt, do for next time.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, August 28.
There was wringing of hands in W-o-o-e among
elders and mothers in Israel when, after parting with
their bawbees, they couldn’t catch half Talmage
said. His big voice tilled the Town Hall well
enough, but in many parts of the building, the
actual words of the yawning smiler were unin-
telligible. Songs without words have a fascination
all their own, and silver-toned Sara Bernhardt
charmed even those who confessed they didn t
grasp an inkling of what she was talking about.
But Mr, R. S. Smythe’s fleeting delusion is a bird
of harsher note and would need to drive all his
alleged wit and wisdom well home to atone for
vocal disadvantages.
A carefully distributed rumour states that the
recent servants’ ball at Government House left
the Hopetoun exchequer poorer by seven hundred
pounds. The bill is understood to be a_ melan-
choly subject with the noble Earl. It isn’t so
much what the banqueters had , as what they took
away, that rankles in his heart of hearts. One
guest, it is said, hired a dress-coat for the occasion
at a cost of 55., which item of fashionable intelli-
gence went the entire round of the ball after the
wearer had completed his tenth adjournment to
the supper-room. Later on the tail-pockets of the
garment came in handy, when the gentleman left
for home with a bottle of champagne in each.
Falling en route, both his prizes were smashed to
smithereens; but it is stated, on good authority,
that he returned and replaced their loss with
interest.
He champagne; At a Melbourne wedding,
where it had been extensively laid on, a lively
Johnnie approached the happy pair after the
ceremony and said to the bride: “Many happy
returns of the day, Mrs. X!” The lady laughed
appreciatively, but hubby glowered and mut-
tered “ Bad form 1” “ Why, darling !” cooed
Juliet, “of course, Mr. Y. doesn’t mean while
you're alive ! ” Harmony was not a bit restored
by this artless remark, and Romeo, who is said to
be morbid in his cups, challenged the jester to
personal combat. Fortunately his new - made
mother-in-law, a lady of infinite tact, fell into
violent hysterics at the thought of parting from
her darling girl, and so saved the situation.
Ethel Haydon sang sweetly at her well-attended
benefit-concert and looked very pretty in her
simple white gown. Nevertheless, the function
fell somewhat flat, I thought. The usual great
masses of garden produce were duly handed up,
but there was no fizz or excitement. Nothing to
make other girls wild with envy or set them all
agog to have their voices tested, as was the case
after the Crossley benefit. Of course audiences
can’t be expected to raise a burst of madness over
every budding prima-donna, but personal friends
and admirers might have displayed a little more
enthusiasm without danger to the roof. I suppose
there’s everything to be hoped for Miss Haydon’s
future. True, she is only a little damsel, and her
voice is not very large, but she has beauty, youth,
health and a certain very fetching air of inno-
cence. Talking of Ethel’s healthiness recalls the
fact that, some time back, while still a stage-struck
school-girl, her fellow budlets scorned her his-
trionic pretensions, on the ground that she was
“ a little dumpling.” Somehow, seminary Cissies
never allow tiiat a friend is pretty, unless she can
slice a cake with her funny-bone, and cut into the
hassock when she confesses her sins.
On dit that the Salon has sworn to have a foot
in the fame of the next Melba, who rises above the
firmament. Keeping this end well in view, the
sisterhood allows no promising vocalist to quit
these shores for Europe without previously lioniz-
ing her. They were greatly in evidence the other
night, and now come weal, come woe, so far as
Ethel Haydon is concerned, the Salon will have
nothing to reproach itself with. At the same
entertainment, a lady elocutionist orated a trans-
lation entitled “The Benediction.” In addition
to the blood and thunder of the original narrative,
the recitation piled on the pathos by retaining a
poetical Frenchified diction. Its inverted adjec-
tives, verbs, etc., suggested those linguistic feats
of the youthful French student, who reads out
slowly and ponderously, “My father has the legs
long !” “ The boy has the hair red !” etc., etc.
Although her first re-appearance had been
promised on the bills, Lady Hopetoun shirked
Daffodil Day at the Town Hall, Possibly the
chastened nose is not yet equal to inhaling the
mixed perfume of a flower-show. Then there is
always a large amount of perfunctory sniffing at
prize blossoms to be gone through by the prize
lady, so, taking one consideration with another, it
was not surprising that the goddess of the chase
stopped away. As a substitute she offered up her
small son, who might likewise have claimed
exemption on account of his teeth. He is shed-
ding his ivories in front, and might easily be mis-
taken for a mere ordinary child suffering from
the effects of a row with the boy next door.
Coolgardie, I hear, boasts a parson, who, begin-
ning his sermon with the words “ Behold, I bring
you tidings of great joy !” was startled by a sudden
upheaval of his male flock. They surmised that
he was about to reveal the whereabouts of a new
alluvial find.
The same good man lately indited a pastoral
letter from the goldfield to the “ Mothers of
Perth,” whom he requested to provide him with a
bicycle that he might more assiduously visit their
sons on the diggings. The recipients have so tut
not responded eagerly. They plume themselves,
X hear, on the fact that thetr boys are not plentiful
at Coolgardie, and they have no wish to
provide a bike for the redemption of youths from
“ the colonies.” Perth draws a line of distinction
between Westralia and “ the colonies.”
However, Lord Hope-if-you-please walked in
the shadow of his vice-regal father, and the suite
grinned in the vicinity. Something amused the
suite. I think they were tickled by the hairy and
intelligent aspect of the patriarchs who led his
Ex. around. A Governor’s rear-guards are never
hairy or wise, and the combination of those two
peculiarities makes them smirk awfully.
As for the show, the decorated dinner-tables
formed the main attraction. Round each speci-
men, women crowded en masse, arguing themselves
hoarse over its merits and demerits. In my
humble opinion, the gem of the collection was
that arranged by Messrs. Webb of Collins-street,
but not intended for competition. The artist had
obtained a delightfully ethereal effect by omitting
the inevitable Liberty silk massed down the
centre. Instead, each clear, delicate glass of
Eerfect daffodils had its own d’oyley of filmy
eliotrope chiffon.
The prize table was cool and pretty, though the
green of its silk and the yellow of its daffodils and
pansies formed a somewhat crude day-light com-
bination. The 2nd prize was carried off by a
melange of citron and heliotrope silk with yellow
daffodils, which also require the softening effect of
artificial light to be properly appreciated.
There were lots of smart frocks to be seen at
“the Daffodilleries.” Two handsome sisters
looked well in simple, but splendidly-cut, gowns
of brownish woollen check. One had the back of
the bodice-yoke embellished by three small leather
straps, while the same trimming appeared on the
front of one sleeve. It conveyed a suggestion of
surgical handiwork, a sort of ornamental “sling,”
in fact, and may have been intended for a delicate
expression of sympathy with the Countess. Rather
the correct thing, nowadays, is to look “ hospi*
tally ” in a picturesque way.
By the way, the wild flowers from Manly
excited much interest, but they were strangers
in a strange land and met with Cinderella-hke
treatment. Heaped pell-mell on a table, tied
up in tight bunches like variegated cabbages,
the poor little things certainly hadn’t a fair chance.
Now-a-days, when only one kind of flower is em-
ployed in a high-class nosegay, there seems no
charm in blossoms of half-a-dozen different scents
and hues wedged closely together. Wild-flowers
are, of course, as a rule, but tiny blooms, and
need judicious treatment, though nothing suits
the “shower bouquet ” so well as small blossoms,
if properly wired. Such arrangement as had
been attempted was worse than nothing. Anchors
and similar devices in red, blue and yellow, with
not a blade of green to break the monotony of the
woolly surface. One posy was surrounded by a
ring of what looked like worms or slugs, hut which
turned out to be an odd, curly, greyish flower.
The handsome heaths triumphed over circum-
stances, and everyone was interested in one
cluster of waratah. I heard several enthusiasts
enquiring for “flannel-flower,” but none seemed
to know whether it grew at Manly or not. It
used to, years ago, but has all been plucked up by
the roots to furnish forth religious flower-shows.

Motto for intending suicides and others
tired of this life—“ Better the hell you know than
the hell you don’t know.”
Kangaroo tendon is coming largely into
use in America for surgical sutures, so much so
that one firm in Melbourne finds it worth while
to export it to Pennsylvania in considerable
quantities.
Sydney Telegraph reports that “ a single-
handed five-card cribbage tournament has been
held at Oowra (N.S.W.) to raise funds for clearing
scrub at the cemetery.” For peaceful, unobtrusive
melancholy, this item ought to take the cake.
The production by cx-Age man Low, at
the Speight-Syme trial, of that highly-compli-
mentary testimonial from Editor Windsor, after
Purves’ furious attack on Low’s career while on
the Age staff, was rather a knock-out blow for the
burly counsel and the defendant. The latter had
no idea that such a document existed. There was
probably a warm interview in the editor’s room
that evening.
Mundi Mundi, the only N.S.W. sheep-
station in which the now defunct Bank of South
Australia had a finger, brought up four parties of
“free laborers ” from S.A. ere it could get a full
board of 33. A Union camp, formed at Silverton,
got hold of the batches as they were dropped at
various points along the line, and in one case argued
over all but one man out of a dozen.
The Italian Government are sending out
6000 starving Sicilians to Massowah, East
Africa. None of them are ever likely to get back.
They will either get their throats cut by the
Soudanese Arabs or die of fever. But if the races
intermarried, what a splendid breed of anarchists
—the fervid blood of the Sicilian crossed with the
fatalism of the Arab !

Assembly, 25 are native-born Australians. There

House.
Talmage believes in the Bible, but has his

way of interpreting Talmage. His is a cash
business.

the N.S.W. Premiership, and G. H. Reid for the
Speaker’s chair. Each now holds the office prog-
nosticated for the other.
Talmage told the Melb. people he once
cured a case of pneumonia by encouraging the
patient to get better. We know; he told the sick
man that they would meet in Heaven.

An ex-parliamentarian of N.S.W, received
by last English mail two declined articles from a
London magazine office, in which enclosure was
the query; “ Mr. Gladstone has gone over this
field ; why, with your opinions, don’t you keep
your work and, consequently, its value in money
in your colony ?”
No judge who ever sat on the Adelaide
Supreme Court Bench has had a family connec-
tion of any kind practising before him, but there
was once a stipendiary magistrate in the city who
was related to nearly every successful barrister
frequenting his court. A too-outspoken solicitor
was sent to gaol for mentioning this fact, but the
libeller still practises, whereas the magistrate long
ago resigned.
Adam Porter, Chairman of the Auckland
(M.L.) Chamber of Commerce, whose death is
cabled, made three fortunes—the first at store-
keeping and gold-buying on the West Coast, the
second at mining on the Thames, and the third at
coach-building. Charles Atkin, his partner in the
big coach-building business, is the son of the man
who was plaintiff in the litigation on which
Samuel Warren based his novel, “Ten thousand
a year.”
Deputy Master Anderson,
of Melbourne Mint, has published
a volume of verse under the title
of “The Agnostic and other
Poems.” The volume is prefaced
by a capital likeness of the official
poet, who, by the way, is one of
the handsomest old men in Aus-
tralia. Mr. Anderson was for
years Gladstonite Member for
Glasgow, and has been chief coiner
for Victoria since 1885. He
ruralises, at intervals, on his farm,
which is somewhere in the out-
lying suburbs; and combines the
joys of handling the red, red gold
with the felicities of raising
golden corn for his suffering
country at eighteenpence a
bushel.

N.S.W. Commandant Hutton, when he
appears at any military function now, has a staff
large enough for a German corps-d’arvide. If
things go on much longer at the present rate the
N.S W. army will soon come to be all staff. The
province might be able to put 3000 combatants
into the field, and the staff consists of a military
secretary, a D.A.A.G., D.A.Q.M.G., artillery
commandant, commanding engineer, cavalry
commandant, artillery staff-officer, engineer staff-
officer, cavalry staff-officer, and aide-de-camp—
enough for 30,000 men anywhere else.

solicitor (who died some years ago) strolled into
court half-seas over, and sat at the Bar table with
his hat on. The Judge snapped like a spring
rat-trap. “Officer, remove that man to for

Henry Norman, a journalist who has lived
in Corea, puts the China-Japan trouble in a nut-
shell :
First of all comes a permanent ill-feeling between
Chinese and Japanese, who have a rooted dislike of one
another. Second, their mutual jealousy as the two great
Far Eastern Powers. Third, there are Japan’s vastly
preponderant interests—population, shipping, trade-in
Korea, against China’s ancient suzerainty and her modern
political control of Korean affairs. Fourth, the rebellion
in Korea, threatening all foreigners, including Japanese,
stands for something, but not so much as has been made
out, for Korean rebellions are not very serious affairs.
Fifth, Japan is exasperated by the decoying of the pro-
Japanese Korean rebel, Kim-Ok-Kyun, from his refuge in
Tokyo, and his brutal murder in Shanghai, winked at by
the Chinese Government. Sixth, Japan is afraid, not
without reason, that China is about to settle her difficul-
ties with Russia by allowing the latter to occupy a port
on the east coast of Korea. Finally, both countries
believe themselves to possess powerful forces of the Euro-
pean kind, and are not sorry to have an opportunity of
showing what they can do with them. This is much
truer of Japan than of China.
Norman rather thinks Japan will have the best of
the tussle, as her armament is up to date, while no
one knows exactly what China’s is worth. Aus-
tralian Chinese take a different view. They
rather like the idea of the Japs, winning the first
round, and point out that China, like all great
masses, is slow to move. But she will come at
the finish, and if her 400 millions once begin on
Japan in earnest, the new Japanese constitution
will be revised once for all, and there will be a
permanent alteration in the geography-books. So
they say.
The professional saviours of Victoria have ceased to
believe in “ putting their shoulders to the wheel.” The
present necessity— vide George Turner—is “ a long pull,
a strong pull, and a pull altogether to bring the colony
back to prosperous times.” Hooroo ! Let Victoria trust
to Turner and the ’Ouse boat.
Dear Bulletin, —That Imperial “ofiicah”
who compared Vic. mounted rifles to “acwowd
of wabble chased by the police,” did not know
much. What did he expect ? However good the
men were, their horses would spoil them. It is a
delusion to suppose that any horse, if strong and
active, will make a cavalry trooper. When I
served as subaltern of a horse-artillery battery
nearly all our re mounts were horses sent from!
cavalry regiments, for no fault except that they
would not keep the ranks. Many would behave
well when halted, or moving at a walk or slow
trot, but when galloping they got crowded, and
became excited, and one of them would ruin ;i
whole squadron. That shows the absurdity of
applying English drill to Australian volunteers.
You must organise the “mob of mounted people”
on quite different principles if you mean to do
any good with them. — Trooper.

“Rolf Boldrewood’s” tardy
acknowledgment to Louis Broke,
published as an advt. in the
Sydney daily press, hardly con-
veys the somewhat startling fact
that, bar padding, “ A Modem
Buccaneer,’’for which'Mr. Browne
has got much kudos, is mostly by
Louis Beoke, whose name is well
known to Bulletin readers. The
experiences are Becke’s experi-
ences ; the narrative is Becke’s
narrative; the thoughts are
Becke’s thoughts. Bold re wood
met Becke a couple of years ago,
when he was hard-up, as writers
have a knack of being, and gave
him a few pounds for the manu-
script of his South Sea trading
reminiscences when supercargo to
Bully Hayes. From one point of
view it was a square deal, since
Becke at the time was friendless
and unknown, and could not dis-
pose of his work to better advan-
tage. With due acknowledgment
of Becke’s authorship, the tiling
might have passed. But the book
is published, without a word of
Becke, “by Rolf Bold re wood,”
astonishing the critics who “never
knew that Mr. Boldrewood had
voyaged in the South Seas.” That
is, Boldrewood trades off Becke’s
brain-work as his own and
pockets the praise. By the way,
Becke’s book, “By Reef and
Palm,” will shortly be published
by Fisher Unwin, of London, in
the “Autonym Series.” When
it comes out, the world will see
at a glance how the milk got into
Rolf Boldrewood’s cocoa-nut.
A Fijian Islander finds an empty kerosene-
tin of great use. He cuts it in two, and stretches
one part so that it overlaps the other a little, like
a lid, and then ties neatly round the whole with
hemp. This is now the national portmanteau of
most of the Western Pacifies.
The Fiji Legislative Council has passed a
buttery resolution expressing satisfaction that
Gov. Thurston’s term of office has been extended
for four years. This is because Thurston, who
was once fiercely opposed to the importation
of blackbirds from the Gilbert Group, has since
sanctioned it.

Mr. ARTHUR GALTON.
(The newly-appointed N.S.W. historiographer.)
Apropos the arming of the police, Jour-
nalist' Haverfield, of Bendigo, used to relate a
story of a digger in the early fifties being brought
before Commissioner Gibson on a charge of not
having a license. Accused was placed with his
back against a low sapling fence, his face towards
the Commissioner’s tent, the “Court” being held
in the open. The prisoner pleaded guilty, but
wished to ask a question. Leave being given, he
enquired if the police were permitted to fire at a
man who had no license if he ran away? “ Cer-
tainly not,” replied Mr. Gibson. “Then I’ll bid
you good morning, sir ! ’ and, vaulting the fence,
he soon ran out of sight.
Let one and all their hopes renew,
And sing and dance and joke,
For Parliament’s a gallant crew
With Turner rowing stroke.
They hit the ripples, fore and aft,
With measured beat and slow —
Speed on, speed on, thou gallant bark,
The country is in tow 1
’Tis true the country drifted back
To troubled waters, where
The outlook was a duty black
And scandal fouled the air ;
But right ahead the sun in pride
Is shining as of yore.
And hard agin’ the stubborn tide
Those heroes tug the oar.
They never pause to look about,
Their strength is never spent
(What would a country do without
Its good old Parliament ?)
The people weep ; their honest hearts
Of gratitude are full,
The darkness of the past departs
Whilst Turner murmurs “ Pull! ”
The bells are ringing merry chimes
And life’s a happy dream ;
The boat is back in better times
Safe anchored in the stream.
God bless the crew that came to save
A country from its fate,
And bless Tom Bent, the cox’en brave,
Who kept the tiller straight!

POLITICAL
POINTS
A certain Wales ex-M.P. was dis-
coursing on his impressions of political life.
“When I was first elected,” he said, “I wondered
what the boys could see in me to return me to
parliament; but when I got down and saw the
ot | ier s , I left off wondering.”
In the present W.A. Assembly there are
3 lawyers, 5 storekeepers or merchants, 6 commis-
sion agents, one ex-parson, 13 farmers or graziers,
one ex-shoemaker, 2 ex-surveyors, one ex-school-
master, and one owner of river “flats” and tug-
boats. The Upper Chamber is equally well
assorted.
The country press is well represented in
g Parliament, but since the death, a decade
ago, of Proprietor King, of the Advertiser, no
one even remotely connected with a city newspaper
has entered the political portals. Editor Bony-
thon has legislative ambitions, but the “ Han-
sard ” contract keeps him outside.
Dear Bulletin, —Be the hint as to Ninian
Melville’s downfall at Waratah having been fore-
shadowed by his wearing ’possum-skin trimmings
on his overcoat. I don’t believe Arthur Griffith s
services will eclipse those of his predecessor, but
the extent of his downfall certainly will if he
doesn’t swear off lavender pants and yellow boots.
—Rock-Drill.
Gov. Kintore’s term approaches its close,
and still nothing has been done as to his successor.
There has been much correspondence between
Kingston and Lord Ripon as to the suggested
appointment of Chief-Justice Way for a year or
more, but Ripon is obdurate, and Kingston s only
course seems to be to refuse to vote the salary of
the Imperial nominee.
“What’s the use of asking N.S.W. ex-
Premier Dibbs on what authority he ladled out
money -bonuses to civil servants before he left office?
He will reply that Parkes did the thing.
What’s the use of asking Parkes? He'll say
Robertson did it. What's the use of asking any
living man in the position of ex-Premier? Me II
prove to you that it was the custom since JN.b. VV.
got her Constitution.”
Price, S.A. Labour member, on the pro-
posed reduction of Parliamentary salaries; “I
was able to live as an honest man before I came
into this House, but if my salary is made much
lower I, and others, will not be able to remain as
honest as we would like to be.” But then the
great object in abolishing payment of members is
either to get rid of the honest men or else to make
them “less honest than they would like to be.”
“ Eight-hours’ Day ” in Adelaide has been
postponed from Saturday, Sept. 1, till the follow-
ing Monday. The Legislature had not the pluck
in old times to give a holiday specially to the
labor bodies, so they compounded with their poli-
tical consciences by permanently postponing the
celebration of “ the Duke of Edinburgh s birth-
day ” from Aug. 6 to Sept. 1. Even now, the
farce is kept up, for the latest Gazette transfers
“the Prince’s celebration from the first to the
third of September '
The Protectionist
Sydney Star now pats
the puling Parkes.
Parkes in return grants
interviews to Stab re-
porters and the garrulity
is leaded out.

Government is going to
issue £750,000 in 4 per
cent, stock, and £190,000
in 5 per cent. Treasury
bills with a six years’
currency, to pay off cer-
tain old loans which fall

years. It is a ghastly
thing to find an Aus-
tral as i an pro v i n ce st a«v i n g
off the public creditor by
borrowing at 5 per shent
—it would be almost
better to go broke at
once. When is the Com-
monwealth coming along
to mop up all these little
p.n.’s, and turn them
into one National Debt
at 3 per cent, interest,
guaranteed by United
Australia? United we
stand at 3 per cent.;
divided we fall at 5 per
shent.
Sample of railway tariff, designed to keep
traffic off the Darling and Murray, whence it
might go to Melbourne instead of to Adelaide:
Mundi Mundi and Thaokaringa. between Cock-
burn and Broken Hill, and much nearer the
former, cart their wool to Broken Hill, an un-
necessary haulage of between 20 and 30 miles,
because if they loaded it at Cockburn (35 miles
nearer the port than Broken Hill) the rail car-
riage would be nearly £1 per ton more than for
the longer distance. The facilities for loading at
Cockburn are about equal to those at Broken
Hill.
“The lawyers, clergymen, and other pro-
fessional gentlemen who prey upon the earnings
of the working men.” — Hogg, M.H.R. of Maon-
land.
How history is made in Sydney. From
Westminster Gazette :
The birthday banquet tendered to Sir Henry Parkes, on
entering his eightieth year, was of unusual interest (says
our Sydney correspondent). The citizen’s card cost a
guinea, yet working men —laborers and artisms call d
o i or wrote to the secretary to know' whether they would
be admitted without an evening dress. On being t Id
that the evening dress was not necessary, they planked
down their guineas. The majority of those who honored
the veteran by their presence and their cheers were young
men, for singularly enough Parkes is still the most
advanced leader in Australia.
That alleged area of salt marsh and useless
sea-beach at Geelong, which certain Yic. M’s. P.
are so anxious to sell to a salt-making co. for £1
an acre, proves to be an even viler joke than was
generally supposed. The land is not marsh nor
waste sea-beach : most of it is good agricultural
soil, which can’t be used for salt-making under
any circumstances. It has a frontage to the
shore, however, and there is a feeble little estab-
lishment down by the edge of the sea where ten
men are partially engaged at salt-making. Some-
thing like 500 of the 630 acres are of no earthly
use in the salt industry, so there has plainly been
some tremendous lying over this little job.
THE USUAL CONCOMITANT.
SOUTH SEA ISLANDER (who has found a bottle of rum on the beach):
“ Where can that missionary have planted himself?
The tenants of the South Australian Com-
pany, that fat absentee corporation founded by
the pious George Fife Angas, which regularly
sweats at least a 15 per cent, div., have unsuccess-
fully petitioned for a 50 per cent, reduction in the
extortionate rents now levied on them. Enraged
at their non-success the tenants lately held a
meeting and invited Johnson and Caldwell,
M’s.P. for their district, and Homburg, M.P.,
who represents an adjoining constituency, to
attend. Strangely enough, these members are
ultra-Tories, and it was interesting to note that
they all dodged the fact that the absentee and
progressive land-values taxes of the obnoxiou 9
Kingston Ministry are the only efficacious
methods yet proposed for bursting up this oppres-
sive monopoly. Someone also turned up the
familiar old gag that “ many of the share-
holders in the eo. were widows who had
bought shares at the high (price, and if they did
not get their regular div. they would starve.” It
may also be pointed out, remarks a Bulletin
correspondent, that one tenant of the co. is
a widow, who, if hep-rent is not reduced, will also
starve, and as she goes to market all the year
round at 2 a.m. and works like a galley-slave, she
ought to be considered as well as the other widows.
To do it justice however, the S.A. Co. has done
much good. It has taught its tenant slaves the
virtues of land-values taxation, and they in turn
are forcing a similar pill down the unwilling
throats of four High Tories of S.A. Assembly—
Horaburg, Randall, Johnson and Caldwell.

No one knows how much can be said on

In Fremantle Gaol the gallows-beam has
three deep ridges across its upper side. These
ridges have been worn by the hangman’s rope in
the scientific “ working off ” of malefactors.
Your fatalist is an awkward soldier to
handle. The Chinese troops in Corea have none
of the “medical comforts” which gladdened the
hearts of Australian Soudaneses, and when they
fall sick they simply suicide by the score and hun-
dred. Judge Madden is wanted in Corea.
The Australian rabbit-exportation industry
is growing. Vic. Agricultural Department is
shinning 20 tons of first-class hares and rabbits to
London The Sydney Fresh Food and Ice Co.
last year got a London order for half-a-million
pairs, but could not obtain them. Last Xmas
Australian rabbits brought in London as much as
4s. a pair.

An aged man named Priest was found
dead a few days ago in a?dam at Bendigo (Vic.),
with his legs tied together and a large dent in his
forehead. The police theory is that he tied his
own legs together, balanced himself on the edge
of the dam, then stunned himself with a hammer
and fell forward. This truly brilliant mental
effort is further adorned with the information
that “ deceased was a wood-splitter.”
One night recently, in the Maoriland
Council, an absent-minded reporter upset his ink-
bottle in the gallery, and the murky contents
poured down over the Hons. M‘Cullough and
Downie Stewart. The one genuine laugh which
has been heard in that dreary assemblage for
many a long day arose when the two blackened
legislators sloped furiously to the door, while the
reporter made wild of apology from his
perch up aloft.

against it. There is not a feather ruffled in his
knightly coat; not the reflection of the shadow of
the likeness of the sign of the ghost of a droop to
be seen in his ex-Republioan crest. i hough a
shattered political world has fallen in fragments
around him, the ruins strike him fearless as
Horace beautifully puts it. Would it be treason
to hint Hat what gives strength to his mind, elas-
ticity to It is step, and colour to his complexion, is

“In connection with the recent murder
by blacks beyond Ninety- mile (W.A.), swift re-
tribution has overtaken the murderer of Mack.
After the outrage the black was rep >rted to have
got away wounded in company with a gin. The
next morning, however, Dixson’s party, when
about seven miles from the scene of the murder,
found the body of the black hanging from a tree, tied
by the legs, into which position he had evidently
been placed before a dozen bullets—marks of which
told their own grim tale—were fired into the
body.”-
WIRE.
The Northern Ter-
ritory electorate repre-
sented by Solomon and
Griffith in S. A. Assembly
stretches from Queens-
land to Westralia, and
contains 523,620 square
miles. Just about as big
as N.S. Wales (310,938
squaremiles), Vic. (87,884
square miles), Tas. (24.330
square miles) and Maori-
land (105,342 square
miles) added together.
Yet Sol. and Griff, are
not greatly puffed up.
Melbourne Univer-
sity is being retrenched
and knows not how to
increase its revenues.
This establishment runs
the highest-paid lectur-
ing-staff south of the
line —and when do these
lecturers say their piece !
At night, when the
thirsty for knowledge can
come and drink? No,
for that would be demo-
cratic, and it might pay.
The professors lecture
by daylight, when the
mass of the population is
at work or looking for
it, and their audience is
an array of barren benches
with two, three, or per-
haps even six, puny
apothecaries or talkative
attorneys - in-embryo to
grope for examination
tips.

the card of the Conser-
vative candidate re-
quested the officer in

To-Day, a little capitalistic organ lately
started in Melbourne, epitomises its principles in
the following choice remark :
The “cheap money” and the National Bank schemes
are two of the most idiotic, criminal, and obtuse pro-
posals ever made in Parliament, and that siysalot. If
they are ever carried into law the workingman will
quickly realise who it is who are going to be the gainers.
Wages are ruled by the rates of interest, i.e., if the
capitalist can get 8 per cent, for his money he can afford
to pay something like a fair wage ; if he is driven to com-
pete with the Government at 3 per cent,, he will have to
adjust his pay-sheet accordingly. Then follows the
starvation wages of Europe. Let every workingman
chew this.
This is evidently based on the theory that the
employer habitually lends put his money on mort-
gage and pays wages out of the interest. Ex-
perience shows that the man who lends money on
mortgage hardly employs anybody, and that the
genuine employer is generally a borrower, who
can’t pay decent wages, either because he is eaten
up by rent or by the 9 per cent, interest which he
has to pay to the mortgagee.
OUR GREAT MEN.
(Respectfully dedicated to Australia's statesmen.)
We call those great who hate all wrong
And brave the despot’s ban ;
We call those great who bravely Hght
And lead the battle’s van ;
We call those great whose word is wise,
Whoso counsel shineth clear.
Whose noble souls in direst need
Would never feign or fear ;
And these (O contradiction strange !)
Our rulers great we call,
Hecause the men who cringe and creep
Around them are so small.
Q.
P. LUFTIO.
Mr. Adrian Knox, the newly-elected M.P. for Woollahra,
N.S.W., opposes payment of members, and is paying his
official salary into the Treasury each month.
That rising young barrister, Adrian Knox,
Only lately elected by popular “ vox,”
Decides that a member should live without pay—
Work for country at night and himself through
the day ;
In a word, his best hours should be given to self,
And the cause of his country be placed after pelf.
But the falsity’s seen of this Knoxious creed
When we think of humanity’s weakness and greed.
He’s as worthy of payment, who forges the tools,
As the user who wields them to serve knaves and
fools;
And the makers of law are as worthy of lure
As the lawyers whom Parliaments’ acts will
inspire.
Common-sense and the patriot’s feelings it mocks,
Dues the movement now headed by Adrian Knox.
I am Tired!
Sufferers from indigestion and liver complaint are
subject to a continued feeling of drowsiness, head-
ache, tired, aching limbs, and a general inclination
to langor. These symptoms are duo primarily to
a deficient secretion of bile, the natural cathartic
of the liver, which, when secreted in insufficient
quantities, permits the coarse and fibrous;particles
of the food and other waste and poisonoqs matter
from all parts of the system to become Impacted
in the duodenum and lower intestines, creating a
very dangerous condition which requires instant
and proper treatment. An experience of five
years with Clements Tonic has proved this remedy
a specific for such complaints, and its universal
success has resulted in. thousands of imitations
being forced on the market; not because they
possess any therapeutic*value, for they are purely
speculative ventures. Sufferers who desire to get
relief must insist on having Clements Tonic. It
acts scientifically, has proved its efficacy in
thousands of cases as effectually as in that of Mrs.
W. Kellond, Bega, N.S.W., who writes on April
26th, 1893:—About two years ago I don’t believe
there was a greater sufferer in the world from
neuralgia ; I believe I tried a hundred different
remedies—in fact, everything that was recom-
mended to ysc, but without being relieved. My
attention was drawn to Clements Tonic and the
number of cases that had been cured by it—cases
resembling mine. By the time I had finished
three bottles of Clements Tonic I was thoroughly
cured of all neuralgic symptoms, and I have never
had a recurrence of the old complaint. I have
recommended it on all occasions. Hoping this
may benefit others who are suffering from the
above complaint. —I am, yours gratefully, Mrs.
W. Kellond, Bega, New South Wales.

The obtrusively-sanctified potentate of to-day is
the Deeming of next week. A N.S.W. society
girl is just now much exercised in mind by the
fact that no portrait exists of a rather good-looking
adventurer for whom the police are hunting with a
robbery-warrant, save one in which he figures side
by side with her own sweet self.
* * 9
One of the Melbourne evening horrors
has a cookery-column, containing three different
dinner programmes from which the good house-
wife can make her selection each returning day.
There is a French menu, an English menu, and a
“Plain Fare,” which is desperately Hinglish, like-
wise. These are introduced with suitable texts.
The Plain Fare text affirms —“ It’s not what you
eat, but what you digest which makes you happy,
healthy and strong.” A sample of the digestible
dinner bills: “Ox-tail. Corned Beef. Suet
Dumplings. Mashed Potatoes. Cabbage. Macca-
roni Pudding.’’ Get that lot across your chest
and you will be happy. The Bulletin idea of an
airy meal is roast zinc with Yorkshire pudding,
parsnips (underdone), parched peas, leather
fritters, and soapy cheese to alleviate the pangs of
death.

S.A. Freemasons are wroth because Grand
Master Kintore neglected a big local function in
order to attend to Gov. and Lady Hopetoun, who
were then visiting Adelaide. They would like to
throw him overboard in favour of little Chief-
Justice Way, who shines at goat-riding ceremonies,
but they are afraid of laying irreverent hands
upon the Deputy Lord’s Anointed. Then, too,
the Milky Way would be curdled at the very
thought of such sacrilege.
• • •
It looks extremely likely that the Win-
deyer clan will shortly monopolise N.S.W. Divorce
Court work. Judge Windeyer presides on the
Bench; his son-in-law, Mr. Ralston, appears in
almost every case, and young Mr. Windeyer, the
Judge’s son, admitted as a barrister a few days
ago, has already made his appearance as a divorce
counsel. In Queensland and Fiji, the practice of
allowing the relative of a Judge to appear before
him as advocate has been stopped.

comes against him in December he will spend
<£2ooo to secure his election. He evidently looks
forward to title-catching in re the promised visit
of the Yorks.

An Australian, reputed
to be worth at least £500,000,
recently went into the shop
of a country draper to buy a
shirt —one shirt. He over-
hauled all sorts and condi-
tions of shirts for half-an-
hour, and at last inquired
the price of one. “ Three-
and-elevenpence.” Then the
wealthy Australian turned
on the miserable draper and
annihilated him with one
fearsome glare. “ Darn your
cheap shirts !” he said, in a
hair-raising voice. “ When
Igo in for an article I like it
good. Show me some at four
bob I”
Sydney Sunday Times nas roused up a
new kind of monster. A little while ago it called
on its women readers to write and say what kind
of husbands they wanted, and the great, aggregate
spinster thereupon rose like one female and sent
in a full description of her ideal mate. They arc
dismally pathetic reading—these letters. So
many of the yearning spinsters would be content
with so very little, and they are so very little
likely to get it, that the world looks even more
desolate after reading the four columns of gush
than it did before. Most of the spinsters want a
big man, “who is strong and healthy,” and big,
stupid, ox-headed men who are strong and healthy
are numerous enough in all conscience. Also,
most of them insist that he shall be economical and
fond of home. After that thm iiverge wildly in all
directions. “ A Cornstalk ” desires “a chemist
with a good paying business who will sell only
first-class goods.” He must smoke a little (but
not cheap tobacco), and be respectful to the aged.
As if a man who trades in hair-dye, bloom of
youth, false teeth, and the like, could preserve
any respect for the aged. “Adrian” wants a
man who will openly admire other women besides
his wife— he ought to be easily found, anyhow.
Another wants a Radical parson, whose mother
lives at a distance. She proposes to save £1 a
week, so that if he dies she can open a little shop,
“and therefore^would not need to marry again
being true to his memory.” One lady wants “a
man who can use his fists ” —not on her, but on
someone else. Evidently the desire to see some-
one else badly battered is a feature in her femi-
nine soul.
There are also urgent demands for “a man with
a good, open face, brown moustache, and hair
parted at the side and brushed off the forehead ” ;
a man “ who will like to be petted once in a while
—perhaps oftener ” ; “a gaol-warder or Customs
officer, who will never be out after 10 at night,
give his wife all his wages, and never ask an
account ” ; and a person “ who wears a ring and
carries a stick said person will be allowed out
two or three a week, but must come home
by 10.30 in a vertical shape, instead of arriving
on all-fours. Also “ Violet ” states openly
that her husband must have heaps of money
and must let her do as she darned well
likes. The rest of the crowd—and they are
many—don’t soar above a plain and simple desire
for a tradesman who is sober and earns £3 a week.
Only one of them all wants a journalist, and as
she insists on him being a sober journalist, the
inference is that she doesn’t want her journalist
very badly. Taking the letters all round, the
general want is a big, good-tempered man who
will be home by ten sharp. One woman, and only
one, insists on a husband who will bring her a cup
of tea in the morning before she gets out of bed.
This lady is_ the most hopeless of the lot. Her
natural destiny is evidently to marry an ancient
wreck with a multitude of strange diseases, and
rise up constantly in the middle of the night to
get hot-water bottles for his cold and horrible
feet.
♦ * *
Dr. Dutton, whilst under examination in
Melb. Divorce Court, received a private letter
from the Judge to remain in Court after the jury
had been dismissed for the day. When that
awful time arrived his Honor advised the pallid
respondent (who evidently didn’t know what was
coming) to abandon all idea of clearing out to
Maoriland with the children before the conclusion
of the case. Dutton had obtained an appointment
at Dunedin, and was supposed to be on the poin’
of a sudden departure.
• * #
More Imperial baubles ! Queen Victoria
is pleased to permit Australian volunteer officers
to wear a new decoration with the cipher
“V.R.1.” Apparently any Governor can shower
these blessings about if any Government chooses
to pay for them. But is it likely that any Govern-
ment will choose, while every politician prays
nightly that an Imperial ring may be soldered
into his own snout?

Little people are having a bad time in
Australia just now. Mad fathers or mothers have
slaughtered their children in half-a-dozen dis-
tricts, within the week. Some madman has wan-
tonly mangled three sleeping infants during the
temporary absence of their now distracted mother,
a widow ; a N.S. Wales J.P. and his wife have
been convicted of treating with most marvellous
cruelty a child in their employ; and a Sydney
man has been caught hanging up his son by the
neck and waist—the latter attachment just to
prevent absolute asphyxia, and so keep the loving
father out of the condemned cell. All those who
have killed their children are evidently mad—in
most cases through poverty following on financial
disaster and want-of-work.
• * #
The meanest man turns up in a Sydney
“ infant ” who ordered a caterer to provide a large
picnic, and, on being sued, successfully claimed
exemption on the ground of being two days under
21 when the order was executed.
* * #
The Sydney churches are angry at Mayor
Manning giving part of the collection from Dr.
Talmage’s charity-sermon to the St. Vincent de
Paul Society. As it is safe to assume the collection
came from Protestant pockets they assert it should
go to Protestant charities.

There is just one little hint of grog in
Archbishop Carr’s big teetotal lottery for St.
Patrick’s Cathedral, Melb. Surgeon Fitzgerald
is allowed to donate a decanter-stand.

Miss Sutherland, the philanthropic Melb.
lady who accused certain ultra-pious kirk mem-
bers of owning houses occupied as bagnios, rubs
the salt into the members of the Presbytery who
have retributively taken away her pass to Zion.
In a recent letter to the dailies she stands to her
charge, and asserts, moreover, that she told the
holy Willies in question of the character of their
tenants. They did not thank her for her pains.

Another Melbourne policeman has been
wounded by a burglar, and the agitation for arm-
ing Robert with revolvers breaks out afresh. But
when a too-zealous constable breaks up an un-
offending person, having a fit in a public place
contrary to the Act, there is no talk of providing
indiarubber skull-pads for respectable citizens.
The police exist to enjoy the protection of society.
# # «
A Ballarat woman has got herself into
trouble by registering a borrowed baby as her own.
Her husband was anxious for an heir, and she
procured one from a neighbour, and presented it
to him as a legitimate article, whereas it was
otherwise. Hubby advertised his luck in the
paper, and was appropriately jubilant until the
law interfered and undeceived him. It is a wise
father who is quite certain about it.
* # #
Parson Talraage, in his lecture on Evolu-
tion, declares, with the dreadful cocksureness of
a well-paid Amurikan preacher, that whoever
doesn’t believe in the story of the Creation as told
in Genesis is “an infidel.” Also, whoever doesn’t
believe in the literal interpretation of everything
else that is asserted in the Scripture is “an
infidel.’’ The Bulletin here rises to say, with-
out any unnecessary discourtesy, that it doesn’t
believe that Talmage believes in Genesis himself.
Also, it would like to put that hoarse expounder
to the test. It has certain ideas of its own as to
the most appropriate way to apply that test. It
would like to have Talmage kidnapped at mid-
night, by two masked men, and shoved into a
marriage covered with crape and drawn by four
black horses. It would drive him along furiously
for a vast distance till it arrived at a lonesome
ruin, and then it would take the great pastor
under a crumbling archway, and down 150 stairs
into a subterranean vault, where one or two
candles shed a glimmering light, and the water
dripped from the rock-hewn roof and fell with an
eerie sound into the pools on the rock-hewn floor,
and where long, hollow echoes reverberated
through black side caverns, and came back in
strange groans out of fissures.
Then it would take off all the preacher’s clothes,
and cut his hair short, and give him a shower-
bath, and dry him with a horse-rasp. When
this was over he would be led, with nothing on
but a misfit bell-topper jammed down over his
ears, and with a broken feather sticking out of the
brim, through a maze of corridors, until at last he
was ushered suddenly into an illuminated cavern
where 43 masked men dressed in red sat in a semi-
circle, with a skeleton and an open grave and a
coffin in front of them. A large executioner—the
very largest executioner who could be got for the
money—would be sharpening a jagged axe on a
grindstone. And the/e we would make the naked
preacher take a dreadful oath on a skull, and
when the oath was finished we would ask him in
a dreadful voice, while the executioner made the
grindstone whirr furiously, and the masked men
all stood up and waved their swords, or their
pickaxes, or their halberts, if he really and truly
and unreservedly believed in the Mosaic story of
the Creation. And down there, in that dank
cavern, Talmage, in a state of absolute nakedness
—barring the misfit bell-topper—rand with his
hair cut to a stubble, would aokribwledge in a
shaky voice that the Mosaic creation was all bosh.
So would almost any other preacher, for that
matter.

A Brisbane publican, charged the other
day with keeping his hotel open for the sale of
drink on Sunday, pleaded that he did it “ because
he was upset, in consequence of the news of the
death of his mother-in-law.” The excuse looked
rather thin, but he got off with a 40s. fine all the
same.
* • «
A youthful amateur swashbuckler, the
holder of many miles of Australian land, strolled
into a Melb. shop with his new wife, where the
latter, taken by some patent preserving-pans, sug-
gested their purchase. “They’ll do to make jam
for the servants,“said the little innocent. “Ser-
vants !” he snorted, giving his moustache a vicious
tug, “the servants don’t get jam at Bargowlah-
nine.”

The average longevity of the Hawkesbury,
N.S.W,, natives has been lowered during the in-
fluenza epidemic. At Windsor, in July, there
were registered 33 deaths of which 30 totted up
2013 years—an average of three-score-and-ten
3ach.

At Brisbane, the other day, a public
meeting was held re the reclamation of the outcast
aborigines of Queensland. Out of 20 or 30 city
and suburban clergymen not one attended. It
was only the Queensland blackfellow who was
being damned by drink, drugs and disease, and
not some Central African nig. who was howling
for the Word and striped stockings.

It is becoming customary with Melbourne
suburban shops to close of the day.

A wealthy Victorian, who makes no secret
of the fact that his spouse has grievously dis-
appointed him in the matter of an heir, offers
<£2oo reward for information as to the originator
of the false report that his wife had, the other
day, presented him with four boys at once.
Nearly a hundred people called at his house, within
a few days, to have “ a peep at the little darlings.’’
• • •
Cheerful accounts of a village settlement
near Kooweerup Swamp (Vic.). The settlers
divide their time between cultivating their own
little holdings and cutting channels to drain the
famous swamp. Eventually they may come
across Joe Clarke’s money which Mrs. Clara
Parker was supposed to be sinking in the mud.
Clara, as we now remember, used to meet Joseph
in the street, and cry “Kooweerup!’’ whereupon
the amiable gentleman backed another bill for
£5OO, or something very like that.
# # •
John De Baun, late of Silverton and
Broken Hill, runs the chief public-house of Cool-
gardie, The Great Western. With the assistance
of a store, bakery, and a well-equipped livery
stable, his average takings are over £lOOO a week.

“ IMITATION
is the
SINCEREST ELATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on
the credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely
a week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all they seem to thrive,
and the paper who hounds them down most
thrives fattest on their ad vertisements and cracks
champagne over each balance-sheet. Clements
Tonic and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped
to deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecessary to further
extol their virtues.
How to Get a Piano or Organ.
YOU, of course, want the highest quality of tone, action,
touch and beauty of exterior you can afford; but
you must have durability. Durability is an indispensable
condition, and however cheap an instrument may appear,
without durability it is worthless. Avoid the cheap
pianos, for which there are agents in every town, who tell
—and often through ignorance believe—that they are
equal to the Steinway or the Erard or some other first-
class maker. Therefore, no matter how big a bargain you
think somebody else offers, investigate

# # #
With a view to the weeding-out of sexa-
genarians, all N.S.W. State school-teachers sup-
posed to be bordering on 60 have received, from
the authorities, a request for full particulars as to
their ages, length of service, etc. The wise-heads
of the Education department themselves, and
also the inspectors, number many in their ranks
who are considerably over the tabooed age.
• • •
Since cheap fares became the order of the
day, passengers on the steamboats between Maori-
land and Australia are a mixed lot. Recently at
dinner-time, in the saloon of the gorgeous Roto-
mahana, a hoarse voice broke the stillness address-
ing someone at the other end of the table. “Bill,”
said this utterance, “I bet I’ll take up more peas
on my blooming knife than you will.”
* * *
A new regulation in N.S.W. Education
Department compels Inspectors, much against
their will, to notify schools of the date on which
they propose holding the annual examination.
Previously, the Inspector used to swoop down un-
expectedly on the school to try and catch the
teacher napping. And he was frequently success-
ful, too.
• * •
Editor Bulletin—Every Sunday morning
the same four women wait for the steamer on one
of Sydney’s watering places. Every Sunday
morning the same young man with a pious, gentle
face and an earnest, upright manner comes and
kisses those four women. They all look better as
they get on the steamer together. He is obviously
a thoroughly good young man, and he kisses in a
pious, deliberate way that is in perfect harmony
with the noble earnestness of his character. There
is no legal objection to the young man kissing
those four women, but, for all that, the ecstatic
twitch of his nostril in the act of kissing fills my
soul with rage and sorrow, and I would like that
young man to kiss them the night before. . . .
It happened many, many years ago. He was a
great red-headed brute, and she the prettiest of
gentle little wives. He used to kiss her in public,
and his nostril used to twitch, I knew the dear
little soul for 15 years before I dared ask her why
she always walked with her hand on her back as
if in pain. Her big eyes filled with tears. “ I’ll
tell you in confidence,” she said. (Gentle women
who cry like to make a confidant of you). “ Every
morning John puts his feet into my back and
pushes me out of bed to get the breakfast.” The
look she gave has haunted me a lifetime. And
now I can never see a man kiss a woman in the
daylight and twitch his nostril without thinking
of that dear, loving little soul with the sore back
and the great red-headed brute with the large
square feet. It makes me feel murderous —Yours,
Joseph Smith Deeming.
According to the Noumea correspondent
of Sydney Daily Telegraph— who was on the
spot and ought to know —the visit of H.M.S.
Orlando was notable for the freezing reception
accorded to Admiral Bowden-Smith and his
officers, as compared with the ovation given a
couple of months back to the Russian cruiser,
Kreiser, when <£4ooo was spent in a hilarious
orgie. The S.M. Hkkald— like the nice, cour-
teous paper it always has been—observes, per
contra, that “ the visit of H.M.S. Orlando proved
highly popular in Noumea.”
• « •
The great German plantation co. is heap-
ing coals of fire upon the heads of foreign whites
and natives alike in Samoa. Latest advices from
Apia state that “ were it not for the great planta-
tions of the German Co., the island would pro-
bably be in the throes of starvation.” What a
lovely picture it would make to see Robert Louis
Stevenson and the Stevenson entourage waiting
outside the big German factory in Matafele to be
fed on keg beef and cold yam by a stout Teuton
with pale-blue eyes and a pitying smile !
• • •
The memorial-card industry is still in full
blast both in Sydney and Melbourne. The busi-
ness is worked on these lines. The morning
papers are scanned for death-notices, and all the
likely ones are promptly set up in type, and with
a more or less appropriate verse of Scripture are
printed in gold on black cards. A dolorous-look-
ing canvasser —you must be dolorous-looking, or
you can’t succeed in this line—takes the “ proofs”
to the six or eight houses of mourning to which
they refer, and he is a “ waster ” if he does not
come away with a pound order from at least 75
per cent, of his clients. A house with a corpse
still in it, and a family all broken up with grief
will often pan out as much as a couple of
sovereigns. There is lota of money in the busi-
ness_or rather used to be—for too many are at it
now, and three canvassers chasing after one corpse
would kill the best scheme on earth.
Apropos, the present writer one morning stopped
on the steps of a Melb. warehouse to offer his
condolences to the proprietor, whose wife had
died the previous day. Two lugubrious-looking
personages, attired in black, were hovering round
evidently anxious to have a word with the
bereaved husband. Whispered the latter, with a
baleful glance at the waiting ghouls, “These two
fellows are gravestone-drummers. I have had
four undertakers, an embalmer, and a printer’s
canvasser here already this morning, and my poor
wife scarcely dead 12 hours.”
Another ghoul incident. A Brisbane labourer
met his death by accident, and the first intimation
that his wife had of her widowhood was an under-
taker’s tout tapping deferentially at the door and
handing her his boss’ business card.
* * *
To check the increasing tendency of
parents to take their children from school and
put them to work before attaining the pre-
scribed age, the N.S.W. Education authorities
have found it necessary to raise the standard of
the exam, required to be passed by pupils who
want to leave school before reaching the age of 14.
The exam, is so stiff now that scholars who have
successfully negotiated the junior exam, have
lately been “ plucked ” in the school examination.
HUCKSTERING.
Now what may come
Of all this row
’Tween Jap. and Chow,
To make things hum ?
If Chow lick Jap.,
We care not how,
Or Jap. beat Chow,
What harm can hap ?
Chow learn to fight ?
Our coast invade?
Fair risk for trade,
So that’s all right.
For beef Jap. buys—
A million pounds—
And up by bounds
Per ton goes rice.
And coal’s a-boom;
So let them snap,
Both Chow and Jap.,
For elbow-room.
And here’s all hail
To Li Hung Chang ;
Let both sides bang—
We’ve coals for sale.
A.E.W.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insuffi-
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps are
sent.
