Nothing of a strikingly novel character
has interrupted the even tenor of “My Jack,” at
Her Majesty’s, during the week, save that the
“revels on the deck of H.M.S. Seagull have been
strengthened by the appearance of Mr. Fred Dark.”
It is one of the favorite delusions of the melo-
dramatic author that the British tar is much given
to revels on the deck of Her Majesty’s ironclads ;
whereas in reality that miserable naval worm is
liable to get 14 days double duty if he is even
found smiling in the presence of a midshipman.
This takes most of the realism out of the “revels,”
still that giddy variety entertainment can pass on
its own merits. Sooner or later, however, The
Bulletin will feel called upon to suggest that the
poor little scuffle in the Soudan is getting about
used up as a dramatic episode, and that the villain
and the wronged and gloomy hero have got lost on
the burning sands quite often enough, and that
the villain has died out there—after confessing his
sins—until his bones must form an unbroken lino
from the Nile Cataracts all the way to Khartoum.
The great historic spectacle of “Henry Y.*’ is
understood to be in preparation.
** * *
Further rumors of the magnificence of
“ Morocco Bound,” which is to occupy Sydney
Lyceum on Saturday, under M‘Mahouian
auspices, continue to arrive. A portrait of
Miss Nannie Harding, the new prima donna,
appears in this issue. Miss Harding looks
lovely enough to run an opera all by
1 eraslf. Also, it is alleged, on unreliable
authority, that the rest of the co. is fully up to
this sample. Further, it is rumoured that in the
matter of high-kicking “ Morocco Bound ” is even
ahead of “Mdlle. Nitouche,” if such a thing is
p )ssible. A further prolonged study of the giddy
opera last-mentioned makes The BULLETIN fear
that it isn’t possible.
** * *
Miss Nannie Harding, whose portrait
appears in this issue of The Bulletin, is the latest
notable acquisition to the Australian opera-bouffe
stage. Miss Harding was engaged by Mr.
MacMahon, in London, to create the part, in
Australia, of Ethel, in “Morocco Bound,” which
goes up at Sydney Lyceum this Saturday.
Although very young, Miss Harding has had
quite a career. She was prima-donna of the
D'Oyley Carte Co. during its memorable tour
through Germany, appearing as Yum-Yum, in
‘ The Mikado,” and Lady Ella, in “Patience,”
in Berlin, Vienna, Leipzig, Dresden, Munich,
&c. This tour occupied upwards of a year,
and after its conclusion the young prima-
donna returned to England and created the lead-
ing rdles in “The Yeoman of the Guard” and
“ The Mountebanks.” Miss Harding is a Welsh-
woman—a circumstance of which she is peculiarly
proud. She was born in Pembrokeshire in 70,
and began to study music at a very early age. She
possesses a marked individuality and a bewitching
piquancy of manner and pronunciation. Nature
hrs exhibitsd a distinct prejudice in her favor, and
it will bo matter for surprise if she does not
captivate her Australian audience at sight.
** * «
Frank Thornton returns to Sydney Cri-
terion on Saturday to play a 12 nights’ season of
“ Charley’s Aunt.” A brief outbreak of the good
old “Private Secretary” will probably follow.
Considering that Sydney has been very short of
comedy for some months past, and making due
allowance for the fact that Frank Thornton is one
of the best comedians ever exported to these
shores, there ought to be plenty of money in the
venture. Mr. L. J. Lohr is manager.
** * *
Miss Maggie Moore reappears at the long-
deserted Sydney Royal, Sept, 15, with “ Struck
Oil ”as the programme. Maggie’s occasional re-
appearance is the one bright episode in the history
of the Royal nowadays. When she comes, which
happens altogether too seldom, the old theatre
fills up and assumes something like its ancient
aspect of prosperity ; and when she goes away,
which happens much too often, it relapses into its
familiar condition of loneliness, and the forgotten
play-bill hanging outside looks like a shirt hung
up to dry on Babel.
** * *
The Barrington variety and burlesque co-
is, so far, meeting with better luck than most of
its predecessors at Sydney School of Arts. Never-
theless, The Bulletin views this kind of enter-
tainment with strictly limited enthusiasm, and
even the appearance of “the fine specialty artist,
Sam Keenan,” doesn’t rouse it from its apathy.
One black “specialty artist ” with a mouth like an
open door is so like another that this paper can
seldom tell one of them from the other, or either
of them from anybody else.
“The Bauble Shop” was performed at
Melb. Princess’s, on Saturday, by the same good
Brough and Bouoicault company which failed to
make a vigorous success of it in Sydney. The
central idea of a Prime Minister’s romantic passion
for a shop-girl is fresh and true. It was in trying
to turn it too far to the purpose of political
satire that the author over-reached himself. A
suggestion that “ The Bauble Shop ” was intended
as a deep joke can be answered by the hard fact
that Henry Arthur Jones never jokes. His four-
act plays are serious attempts to achieve the sub-
lime, and he steps over the border-line uncon-
sciously. An author who, in “Saints and
Sinners,” could allow someone to say, “If our
Queen knew of your conduct she would tear those
epaulettes from your shoulders with her own fair
hands,” has certainly no keen sense of the
ridiculous. He damaged “ The Middleman ”by
trying to put forward the easy victim of a weak-
minded young man as a heroine. His “ Dancing
Girl ” was the work of a mind that sees natural
greatness in dukes, as dukes, and its momen-
tary London success lay in the lamentable circum-
stance that the public mostly shared his delusion.
There has been something seriously the matter
with each of Mr. Jones’s plays, for the reason,
perhaps, that he has never been able to laugh at
himself. “The Bauble Shop” runs for a week
only, and on Saturday they set up Pinero’s
“Cabinet Minister.” Messrs. B. and B. are
striking the local political iron while it is hot.
• • • *
By the way, a great theatrical event hap-
pened at the Princess’ on Monday of the present
week. The refreshment bars passed into other
hands, and the incoming management provided
new properties and effects, together with an
interesting change in the female cast.
** * *
“ Missing at Lloyd’s ” is still revelling in
its great marine disaster at the Alexandra. Dan
Barry’s advertisements don’t do much more than
full justice to this ingenious series of spectacles,
which are realistic and billowy, and altogether
more imposing than the average Alexandra sensa-
tion. The last chapter in the shipwreck, con-
cluding with a rescue of two weary fragments by
a vessel, is a credit to the stage-carpenter
and a triumph for the unseen forces that agitate
the broad expanse of ocean.
** * *
At the Royal there is a good deal of ship-
wreck, and George Darrell rushes forth to the
drowning passengers in a tub that only George
would venture to navigate. The tub contains a
mast and sail when it puts forth from the shore,
but for the return journey George plants himself
and the heroine in the place where the sail ought
to be, and the wind carries them along just as
rapidly. Meanwhile, a line has been fired to the
schooner by a rocket. At brief intervals
a wet seaman travels violently down the
line by means of a sliding hook, and is
restored to consciousness at a coke fire. Judging
from the slant of the rocket line the sinking
craft must be 100 yards above the level of
the sea. Another rousing novelty in “Hearts of
Oak ” is a dastardly attempt to blow George to
pieces with a dynamite-cap. On this occasion, it
is the heroine who preserves George. Perceiving
his white, insensible face in the moonlight, with
the fuse smouldering away behind his right ear,
the maiden swings herself across a chasm, trips
daintily along a rocky path, and throws the
dynamite down a precipice, whereupon there is a
fearful explosion in the bowels of the earth, and
some artful person throws masses of blasted rock
upwards just to show what would have happened
to George if the young lady had not intervened.
On Saturday they revive “The Sunny South.”
** * *
Fillis’s Circus has broken out again in
Melbourne at a new place, the Feeley Family and
all other things being as good as ever. Also, the
annual time has arrived for an Agricultural Show,
and the variety companies are laying themselves
out to catch the horny-handed producer on the
hop, and send him back to his rural home with »
chorus on the tip of his furred tongue.
** * *
It is hardly necessary to repeat that Miss
Violet Yarley (Mrs. Joseph Tapley), whose por-
trait is printed in this issue, made her first
acquaintance with the stage as a Pollardian
juvenile. The petite lady was still in her teens
when she attained the dignity of a place among
the principals in the “ Firm’s ” famous company,
and became associated with Miss Flora Graupner
in leading parts. It was whilst singing in “The
Old Guard ” that Miss Varley’s fresh young
voice charmed Sarah Bernhardt, and earned
the tribute of the tragedienne’s bouquet.
More recently the subject of this notice
made her biggest hit as La Cigale, playing and
singing the prima-donna character to the delight
of audiences in Sydney, Melbourne, and else-
where. Miss Varley’s most particular achieve-
ment during the past few months has been her
marriage with Mr. Joseph Tapley, a oomnetent
critic who shares the general belief that his pretty
little wife has many operatic triumphs in store for
her.

Dear Bulletin, —I attended Talmage’s
last Melbourne lecture, “School for Scandal,”
and, out of fifteen jokes, one struck me as brilliant
and quite new. Crowding out, I heard a venerable
man, with Parkesian hair, complaining about the
resurrection of that one “ new ” joke, with the
bitterness of a worldling who has struck an oyster
of last year’s picking. — Yours, Snell.

difference the Lyric club’s performance at
Melb. Alexandra recently, and the Melb. Opera club’s
performance of “ The Gondoliers,” at the Bijou, was that
the young ladies of the former showed more of their upper
storey, to the great satisfaction of the gods, while the
young ladies ol the latter displayed, to the utmost delight
of the baldheads in the stalls, more of the lowerstorey. It
was al 1 bust with one club, and all limbs, encircled with
fleecy lace and embroidery, with the other. Plump and
shapely limbs they were, of which the pretty Melb. Opera
club damsels should be highly proud. The Lyric club
girls, whose modesty would not allow them to display
their nether limbs to advantage, were, such is the force
of fashion, almost bare from neck to waist.

Rickards is the marvel of the music-hall stage. At
fifty-eight years of age he has much more voice, “go”
and magnetism than any other Australian performer in
the same line. If he were to re-appear on the London
'all stage, with two or three strong new songs and the
reputation of being as old as he really is, 'Arry would
make a lot of the reigning “ stars ” look small. Rickards
Redivivus ! He would need to be properly explained and
set forth on the programme, so that a decrepit, alcoholised
Cockney every here and there among the audience might
state to the youngsters that this was a man who had
outlived five-sixths of the 'Arries who heard him when he
first came out. As for his rivals of those days 1 And how
to account for it I Rickards has the vitality of his race.
He is of the Chosen.
“ No painter, writer, orator, who had the power and
judgment of a thoroughly cultivated musician, could
sin against the broad principles of good taste,” says
Philip Hamerton. Yet the Marshall Hall-ite, who
ventures to write or orate frequently, does sin against the
broad principles of good taste, a professional musician
being too often a professional prig, if not an unmitigated
snob. His intellect feeds on musio alone, whereas
Hamerton speaks of a man who takes the art medicinally.
Are the long-haired cranks listening?
Talmage gave four lectures in Melbourne. An extra
three would have sufficed to play him down to empty
benches.

Rev. John Johnson, Limited, capital, £60,000 in 500
shares of £lOO each ; of which 300 will go to the pro-
moted.
“Martha” writes to the Argus about Talmage’s
lecture that an apparently inebriated gentleman left
noisily by the hack door before the lecture was over,
loudly declaring his opinion that it was rot. “Those who
heard him,” she adds, “looked gratefully at him for ex-
pressing their own sentiments so tersely.”

During her season in Adelaide, Sara Bernhardt turned
round suddenly and said, in French, of course: “My
God lit is cold ; bring me a cloak.” And it was brought.
So naturally was it all done that very few of the audience
knew that the incident had nothing to do with the parti-
cular tragedy being enacted.

Troubles, like snakes, should always be
faced, and a cheerful and prosperous exterior is
always best, however hard the fight. There is a
public benefactor in this city who has done much
to give that creditable appearance which, to quote
him, “is better than bank-notes and as good as
gold.” Those who seek him go forth arrayed in
the latest and glossiest of hats calculated to secure
an overdraft even on landed security. His name
shall be nameless, but his initials are Priddy the
Hatter.*
The yarn that a man and his wife and
seven children were lost for a month in Anthony
Hordern’s lacks confirmation. It was said that
they came down from the wild bush to see the
great city of Sydney, and, arriving by night, they
walked straight across from the railway station
into Anthony Hordern’s, and thought that was
Sydney. So every day they did some shopping
at a fresh counter, watched the crowds of people
making purchases, had their meals in the restau-
rant, and slept by night in the bedding and bed-
steads department. It was only when their holiday
was over, and they wanted to go home again, that
they found they had never been out of the great
Haymarket Emporium all the time. A glance at
Anthony Hordern and Son’s new catalogue makes
one wonder whether this story is so impossible as
it looks. Literally millions of articles of all
descriptions are noted in the 480 pages of good
things which more than justify the firm’s title of
“ Universal Providers.” As business literature,
this catalogue is the most remarkable thing ever
issued south of the line, and speaks of an immense
expenditure of brains and method. The firm
which has produced it, as one of the chief sources
of Australian industry and enterprise, has every
reason to be proud of such ,a monument of its
success.
The story brief of a statesman grave
The son of an ancient earl,
Who came to grief when he oddly gave
His heart to a toy-shop girl.
Though swayed at first by an evil whim
He shrank from his purpose vile,
For the toy-shop lady converted him ;
But her saintly boss, with whiskers grim,
Was hovering round the while.
’Tis awful to think that the seraphim
Are Hard-shell persons, with whiskers grim.
Who hover around the while.
The statesman lord and the damsel fair
Took tea in the shop one night;
So the saint sneak’t ip and found them there.
And brought all the facts to light.
The scandalous narrative, half untrue,
Admitted of ne’er a doubt;
The statesman’s party was left to rue
His folly in doing what all men do
And getting himself found out.
It was not immorality, bold and “blue,”
But “some damfoolish thing which all men do,”
And he was the man found out.

A Bourke (N.S.W.) man, who was “saved” through
Talmage’s sermons, journeyed to Sydney to see the man
who had oped to him the pearly gates. Talmage gave
him an audience, and the story goes that when the owner
of the salvaged soul took himself off, the circus divine
seemed quite relieved. “ These sort of interviews.” he
said, “ generally wind up with a request lor a free pass to
the lecture.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Nine years a pro, pony-racing around Sydney
only furnished £lOOO in prizes. During the year ended
31st July last, the amount had increased to £26.720.
* * » »
There is but one trouble ahead of the totalisatoi
in S.A.—the clubs are beginning to overdo it. The S.A.
clubs are the richest in Australia, as they have big
balances, whereas in the other provinces the clubs are
either in debt or only just solvent. Already M’Gillivray,
M.L.C. (S.A.), is anxious to secure the tote for the Port
Adelaide club on the ground that it is as much en-
titled to it as the others. It was this very argument that
led to the abolition of the machine in S.A. in 'B4.

Tommy Corrigan’s best friend was Martin
Loughlin. Martin informed the writer that he bad
twice arranged with the jockey to take the pledge,
but that Tommy did not come up to the scratch on either
occasion. Martin dropped racing mainlv on this ac ounl.
He told the writer that he would trust his life to Tomnn
when “off it,” but when he lost Corrigan he gave racii g
best, as “ there was no other man to whom he could trust
his money over the sticks.”

The Melb. benefit-meeting to Corrigan’s widow
did not pan out nearly so well as was expected; many
who should have been there were absent. However, it
was a mistake to think that as much enthusiasm would’be
shown over Corrigan dead as had been done over
“Augur” Chapman alive and able to write. Even with
sporting men gratitude is in some sort a sense of favors
to come—and poor old “Augur” had a nice way of
noticing people in his “ Under the Elms ” column.

Corrigan, Burke, Regan, Nolan, Bolger—how
Irish the cross-country riders are I
* * * «
The Melbourne detectives who have in hand
the case of an absconding clerk say that they have
had scores of offers from spielers to hand over the
absconder within six hours provided each of them “ gets
a bit on account." The most persistent of them happens
to be a man whom the absconder assisted monetarily
(about three months ago) to keep him out of prison.
• * • »
For heroic self-sacrifice commend m to the
advertising sporting tipster. One of the fraternity who
announced only a fortnight ago that he had put his
“clients on to every good winner since last Melb. Cup,”
has just filed his own schedule. Liabilities, £llOO ; assets,
nil. No doubt he purposely backed stiff-’uns himself to
make a good market for his customers.
• • * *
Four years ago 150 Melbourne bookies had
£lOOO volumes on the two-Cups double. There may be
500 who have the same this year, but there might be a
hitch in collecting that amount from at least 460 of them
* ♦ * ♦
It takes a racehorse to carry 12st. up the hill at
Moorefield, and then smother his field in the straight as
Ballater did on Saturday last in the Welter Handicap.
Gallater and Kelso are a combination hard to beat.
• • * ♦
Waltzer’s people were before the stewards at
Moorefield, on Saturday, over the Tramway Handicap
running, but explanations were satisfactory. The crowd
watch Waltzer closely, for they have an idea that some-
day that neddy may be in the humour. And the idea
obtains that the day Waltzer is in the humour the rest of
the field may as well stay at home.
• • » *
N.S.W. trotting owners have only themselves to
blame for the fact that whilst the sport is really taking a
hold in Maoriland and Victoria, it is completely dead
about Sydney, Lilliebridge being the only ground which
now risks a trot. At Aspendale Park, Vic., the other day,
the consistent Osterley won the Handicap Trot in the mag-
nificent time of 5.2-J for the two miles.
♦ * * •
The purchase of Capstan by W. T. Jones is a
food augury for that horse's backers for Caulfield Cup.
robably “Bravo” got sick of seeing so splendid a
chance of another big Cup being fooled away in pursuit
of tinpot stakes. Burnett was said to be putting the pro-
ceeds of each of these little wins on the son of Sheet
Anchor for the Caulfield. But what a way to back a
horse, and what a style of training a would-be Cup
winner 1 Very probably Mr. Jones had already a big stake
on the horse.
THE MODERN ARENA.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
And here the buzz of eager thousands ran.
And tuneful laughter rippled up the tiers
Of rustling stands. A Title, in the van,
With Beauty, Wealth and Fashion for compeers.
'Tis Caulfield, and attendant hopes and fears
Burn high where snowy bosoms rise and fall.
The stirring history of the vanished years
Will now be re-enacted. Each and all
Must play their part once more in this day’s carnival.
“ Up with the rag,” and see all eyes intent
Where yonder colors form a silken skein ;
Now, in prismatic-hued confusion blent,
They disentangle and unite again.
Then sweep away in order, and the strain
Draws tightly at the heart, and, Ah 1 a thrill,
A gasp, a shudder as of actual pain.
The favorite wins it 1 ’Twas a nasty spill 1
But men like those, you know, are difficult to kill 1
Yes, raise him tenderly, he struck the rail;
The horse took off, a moment in advance.
Some water, someone 1 He’s so deadly pale !
Stand back there, you, and give the man a chance !!
There, lift him gently in the ambulance—
Another jockey meets a jockey’s fate.
Make way a moment, and then—on the dance I
No truer horseman ever topped a gate
Than he who now abides the final Steward’s weight.
’Twas not unpleasant when emotion thrilled
And pitying interest dimmed sweet eyes with brine;
I saw one point where Nicholson was killed,
And caught the whispered name of Silvermine,
“ Sic transit gloria." See anotherline
Of eager horses marshalled for the fray 1
Hark to the thundering shouts 1 Whose colors shine I
The favorite wins 1 1 The favorite all the way 11
And brings to Joyous close a sportsman’s holiday.
B. CAULFIELD

W. L. Kerr’s win of the Mile Bicycle Cham-
pionship at Redfern Bicycle Club and Sydney Hamers
sports on Saturday was an easy one. A great race was
expected between Kerr and Parramatta Simpson but
Simpson did not show his Brisbane form, and
beaten by Alphonso Brown for second place. Kerr beat
the previous grass-record 2‘33, his own, by 1-5 second.
* ♦ * *
In the foot-racing, Sydney was simply “ iot in
it’’-the local clubs’ only good showing being in the 120
yards Scratch Team race, when Hill finished first, although
Melbourne won the contest on points. Hdls time was
12 l-ssec., which beat Richards’ previous best Australian
record (amateur) by 2-ssec. Davis, of Melbourne, a
magnificent hurdler, was the hero of the day.
* * • •
Davis, who won the hurdles at Sydney on
Saturday, running 135yds. over ten 3tt. 6in. in
ISiseos., is a natural hurdler, better even than Bob Lusk,
and probably very near Batger’s form, if extended. He
takes his fences in his stride and was never pushed on
Saturday. The quarter-mile showed the Victorian,
to be an all-round good man, for, with two hurdle heats in
him, he ran home an easy winner by syds. in 53 l-2seo.

is officially credited with running lUJyds. in 11 9-lOsecs.
in the final, only 4Jyds. worse than evens on newly-top-
dressed grass. J. Hill, of Sydney Harriers, is similarly
credited with 2yds. worse than evens, v.e., 12 1-5 for
the full distance. As he only won by a few inches, people
are asked to believe that Davis and Shaw are at least up

Sculling champion Stanbury is still living at
Rvde. He has very little indeed to do, and is much dis-
gusted at the perfidy of Englishmen Bubear and Harding.
They gassed that they had £5OO to match the latter
against big Jim, but when the champion’s agent went to
England, ready to cover it, they were found unable or
unwilling to raise as many shillings.

The beating of the N.S.W. team of Rugbeiana
by second-rate Auckland augurs ill for its success in any
but very small matches in Maoriland.
« • • *
lb is within the law, bub hardly fair to real
amateurs, to allow the three-mile record of 15.27, by
Manrilander Burk, to stand. Burk was for years a noted
M.L. pro., and it was only when he had nothing to con-
quer that he got admission to the amateur fold.

A Sydney evening paper lately sent a circular
to the N.S.W. Rowing Association offering a big trophy
for a double-scull race in club skiffs, open to amateure in
the Australias, the race to be called an Intercolonial
contest. And now the joke comes in. Association
patronage was asked for, the only stipulation being that
Messrs. Moor and Waters, of the North Shore Rowing
Club, should be barred from rowing in the same bo it. To
call the advt. an intercolonial contest, aud bar the crack
Sydney crew, bangs Bannagher.
Wool and Produce Reports.
M‘Burney, Wallis & Co. report:—This being wool-
sales week, several lots of new clip passed over to buyers
under the hammer at prices in excess of this time last
year As vet the new wool is sound and well-grown, but
in some cases lighter in the grease than last year. We
have the pleasure of seeing our prediction verified, and it
is now evident to all that we are to 1 a-e a happier state
of things in our wool auction-room this season, ihe sales
were attended by the largest number of buyers ever
known for the time of year. Although several foreign
buvers are again with us, we have a number yet to arrive,
and it is reasonable to expect a still further inorease in
price when the full buying strength of the world, America
included, congregates to bid for the chief product of our
country. Apart from the free admission of wool into the
United States, trade is becoming more animated in England
and on the Continent. The cloud of depression which lately
enveloped the whole world is lifting and the time is close
when business will once more be conducted in the sun-
shine of prosperity. Although prices are likely to be
better this year we would caution owners against hamper-
ing their broker and prejudicing the sale of their wool by
placing excessive reserves upon it. Select a reliable
broker and put your faith in his judgment is the best
advice we can give on this point.
Auer. 25th, 1894.
New Zealand Loan & Mercantile Agency Co., Limited,
report Wool : The sales held during the past week
attracted a very large attendance of the trade and passed
off satisfactorily, prices being a shade firmer, especially
in the case of scoured wools which sold at up to 14a.
per lb. Several lines of the new clip were brought for-
ward, and for these wools prices slightly in e xcess of last
year's currency were, in most instances, forthcoming.
For the CWB over ROYSTON mark from the DarhngDowns
we received an offer of 7d. per lb. for the best fleece, and
at this figure the parcel was withdrawn. Our latest cable
message from London reports a fractional advance in
values for sheep-skins, and we anticipate that the Sep-
tember wool-sales in London will open at an advance on
July closing rates.

The Sale of a Landlord,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Somebody was stealing the palings. Day
by day the gap at the bottom of the yard grew
larger. The palings were being carried off in the
night. The landlord was angry. He questioned
the man who lived in the house— such a house, too,
a poor, narrow, miserable-looking little cottage,
badly-built,_ badly-drained. But the tenant could
throw no light on the mystery. He said it was
strange, and hard on him.
“ I’ll watch,” he said.
But his watch brought no evidence to light. The
stealing still continued.
“I'll watch now,” said the landlord.
For three days and nights he kept a guard, he
and his two sons. On the third morning they
made a discovery.
It seemed that_ the tenant himself got up early,
just past the middle of the night, and pulled up
the palings to light the fire with. They caught
him in the act.
Forthwith a summons was taken out and served
on him.
“ Miserable wretch !” said the landlord, a pro-
sperous trader. He was sitting in his office next
day when there came the sound of footsteps round
his door. Somebody knocked.
“ Come in,” he called.
Then the door opened and a procession came in.
The tenant first, a baby in his arms. Then a
woman—his wife, no doubt —also carrying a child.
Then one, two, three, four, five, six, seven chil-
dren. They darkened the doorway.
“ What on earth !” said the landlord.
It was about the summons they had come, ex-
plained the tenant. He was woefully out-at-
elbows, and had a hand-to-mouth look generally.
So had his following.
There was nothing to be done, said the other.
They must go away and not come bothering him.
Attend at court when the thing came on. That
was all he had to say about the matter.
So they trooped out dolorously, the tenant and
his burden, the wife and hers, and the seven
draggled children. It was several minutes before
the echo of their footsteps died away. They
haunted the Landlord for quite a time.
• « •
The case came duly before the magistrate.
The landlord remembered to be there. The
tenant would like to have forgotten, but dared
not.
And he came as before with a body-guard. He
came with the self-same infant in his arms, the
wife behind him with a little one in her arms, the
long train of miserable-looking children bringing
up the rear. There was quite a sensation in court
as the sad procession trooped in.
“Poor wretch,” said the landlord. He had
meant to be hard, but this was harrowing. He
was ashamed of himself. He whispered to the
magistrate that he did not want to follow this up
too harshly. Be as lenient as possible. And in
the end the tenant was let off with a shilling tine.
“ We had no money to buy wood, and no food,
nor nothing,” he pleaded.
And the landlord found himself paying the
fine.
“It doesn’t do to be too hard,” he said.
“ There’s such a thing as measure for measure.”
He gave the tenant ten shillings, and told him he
must look out for another house as soon as he
could.
He had meant to send him out that day—five
weeks’ rent was owing. But those nine children
made havoc of his resolutions.
He told his wife about it when he got home.
It seemed, then, that she knew the people well.
She stared at him for a minute.
“Nine children/ Why,” she said, “they’ve
only got three 1“
And she was right.
M.L.M.
“Indecency is a matter of longitude and
latitude. In the East it would be regarded as
indecent for a woman to go about with her face
uncovered. In Victoria it is not regarded as
indecent for a woman to go into a ballroom only
half dressed.”— James Smith in the “ Ovid ”
pi j. e.cution.
Premier Reid says : “ Now I tell you that
every penny that travels into one man’s pocket
must have come from the pocket of another man.”
Must it, indeed ? Then how did the first man who
ever wore a pocket—the son of the last man before
pockets were invented—come to have any money ?
And where did the first penny come from ? Also,
how is it that there is twenty times as much
money in the world as there was some centuries
ago ? Again, if no man can have any money save
what was previously in the pocket of another man,
then the chain of reasoning goes right back until
it is distinctly proved that all the money on earth—
nearly 40,000 tons of it, at the lowest estimate, not
reckoning paper money and Parkes’ cheques—
must have been in the pocket of the first man, and
how on earth he could get round after victuals
under such a load passes comprehension. Is Mr.
Reid prepared to face thisinevitableconclusion, and
does his creed allow of a first man with 40,000 tons
of money in his pocket ? Further, is Mr. Reid pre-
pared to assert that gold and silver habitually
grow in pockets and don’t come out of the ground ?
Lastly, as his theory distinctly implies that wealth
can’t be increased—in other words, that the world
can’t become any richer than it is, no matter what
fiscal policy it follows, then what is the good of
Freetrade? Also, what is the good of anything?
The Bulletin intends to follow Dibbs’ plan and
send these queries along to Mr. Reid by a special
messenger, who will be instructed not to come
away without an answer, even if he has to sit on
the Premier’s door-mat like an accusing spirit for
70 days.
Dear Bulletin, —lt wasn’t so much the
miners of Waratah who rejected Melville as the
railway men. Miners are more constant —witness
the return of Freetrade Fegan by Protectionist
Wickham. Not so the railway men. When the
railway men of Redfern embraced Protection and
tribune Sohey failed to profess it, he was rejected.
Six months afterwards, when he did profess it, he
was returned. The railway vote (I don’t mean
the railway civil service vote) was everywhere a
Labour one. The railway constituencies returned
Labour members, the railway dep6ts in the
country supported Labour candidates. Which
shows what an excellent army the Labour leaders
have failed to organise in the railway men.
Numbering 10,000 or more in N.S. Wales, the
Australian federation of railway men should be
the third or fourth strongest union in Australia,
and that factor in the destinies of Democracy that
the railways are in the destiny of Australia.' But,
alas ! it is not yet. Holman had a paid position
as secretary of the discredited R.T.E. Associa-
tion, and left it worse off than he found it. But,
of course, it was beneath the dignity of young
man Holman to be the apostle of the railway men
—the W. G. Spence of the railroad. He preferred
to be the W. A. Holman of the Central Com-
mittee. — Lag oon.

This Syndicate is being formed to acquire from the present holders Gold Mining Lease No. 533,
situate at White Feather, Coolgardie, W.A., known as McKenzie’s, and having an area of 15 acres.
This property immediately adjoins M'Auliffe’s Lease, No. 156, which has been recently purchased for
an English Syndicate for a very large sum, and the following extracts from the S.M. Herald will
give some idea of its great value
“Mr. H. J. Saunders returned last night from a lengthy inspection of the properties secured by the Western
Australian Goldfields Company. He is well satisfled with the mines, especially with JTAuliffe’s Day Dawn, at White
feather, in which wonderfully rich gold was struck at a depth of 12ft. in the new shaft in the centre claim. The
southern shaft is down 60ft., and shows heavy coarse gold all the way.”— Sydney Morning Herald, Wednesday,
July 25, 1894.
“ At White Feather gold has been struck in a claim locally known as belonging to Lord Percy Douglas, purchased
for the Western Australian Goldfields Company, Limited. It bears the name of M'Auliffe’s claim, and is said to be
richer than Bayley’s or Londonderry.”— Sydney Morning Herald, August 11, 1894.
The cost of maintaining property from 12th inst. to be borne by the Syndicate ; any gold obtained
during this period to be the property of the Syndicate.
Reports on the property by Mr. W. H. Gorbould, M.A.1.M.8., and Messrs, Robert Pettigrew, J.
Treloar and A. Goldie can be inspected at the office of the Syndicate, 131 Pitt-street, Sydney.

The German Kaiser, who keeps a special
photographer, is constantly being photographed,
and the artist has to turn him out “real nice” every
time. The apparatus can't lie, but the re-toucher
is the greatest perverter of truth on earth.
The record tumour was recently removed
from a female patient at Glasgow (Scotland)
Hospital. It weighed more than half as much as
the woman herself. No tumour of the size has
been successfully removed before, or, if it has,
there is no record of the performance.
“Genius is more common among men
[than women] by virtue of the same general
tendency by which idiocy is more common among
men. The two facts are but two aspects of a
larger zoological fact—the greater variability of
the male.”— Havelock Ellis in “Man and Worn'*'" ”
(1894).

A Sydney vegetable “John ” philosophises
on the hard times. “ Big house no good—too
muchee grand. Lady bang-bang panna alle time.
No pay you. Say, ‘ call smother day, John!’
Welly ni’ way, and welly polite, but no pay you.
Next time she say, ‘ tomollow ; ’ then tomollow
come she tell you, ‘call smother day.’ Bah !no
good big house. No money! Lillie house no so
bad. Pay liHie thisse day ; some notha time.
Getta money allee same byemby. Welly bad
times, by cli ! ”
A very well-known N.S.W, police-othcex',
now high up, had a narrow escape. When a
trooper, he happened to gain the envy of a certain
bush publican, ac whose house he put up one
night “weary.” Seeing the officer “overcome,”
Bung attempted a coup de main. He stole a
lodger’s watch and chain and planted them in the
trooper’s boots ! The scheme failed, inasmuch as
X. was up first in the morning and found
the plant, on returning which to the landlord he
placidly explained that want of direct evidence
alone prevented him from prosecuting Bung for
the theft.
In Newfoundland, the Premier(SirWilliam
Whiteway), most of the Cabinet, and about half
the Ministerial party have been ejected on petition
for bribery and corruption. Apparently the fish
province is about as corrupt as Canada, and
Whiteway is in a fair way to be another grand old
man of the Macdonald pattern. Both Canada
and Newfoundland are, as a general thing,
disgusting and crawlsomely “loyal,” and this
latest development makes it almost look as if
loyalty and swindle were much the same senti-
ment.' See how loyal Victoria stinks !

Dear Bulletin, — If “H. J. Mac.” isn’t
starting a lie - competition, his latest cantan-
trurn (copyright!) mayn’t be so wide of the snake
business, after all. That Saga of “ Wild Fer-
guson ” has a fine jarnboristio flavour about it,
anyhow, which suggests that where the common,
nickel-plated boozer “sees snakes” the gold-
mounted M’Cooey “gets navvies.” And was it
“artistic restraint,” or a lucid interval that made
him omit to describe how the Osdanic Ferguson,
having feasted on the slain, by the light of history,
even unto seventy times seven, ascended to the
bottomless pit in a chariot of tanglefoot. All which
is not greater nonsense than the assertion that a
navvy is never a man. There are plenty of
navvies who could give the eminent M’Cooey
points in fair-play and common-sense, and who
have nothing but physique in common with the
Yisitation-of-God hero who finds his Homer—or
his Munchausen—in the wild-haired Apostle of
the Adder. —A.M.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Editor Bulletin. —“Jerry” is not quite
correct about Sydney charities—notably the
Labour Bureau and Benevolent Asylum. With
regard to the latter, its one inspector, having been
at the work for years, is worth all the three
Government-stroke men of the former. The B. A.
is practically a Government institution, and, as
the two work into each other’s hands, double-
banking, such as “Jerry ” hints at, is out of the
question. Further, the L.B. only gives food, and
that only to the value of Is. s|d. a week, to each
family—no fuel, salt, pepper, or vegetables—and
that is given in the shape of orders on a local
grocery firm. If the husband is, in the opinion of
the L.B. officials, unable to work, he is sent over
to the B.A. across the street, and vice versa.
Now, all this is utter folly. It is demoralising. It
would surely be easy to give these poor people a
chance to earn the little they get, if it were only
to set them sawing firewood.—R.

An organ-grinder and monkey have
reached Coolgardie. The latter so alarmed the
niggers, that for days nothing would induce them
to go within 100 feet of it, then fear gave way to
laughter. The tail amused them most.
James Tyson kept an account for 25 years
of the profits he made out of the progeny of one
brindled heifer, which he had acquired by swap-
ping a flute. The total, he says, was over .£70,000.
The Bulletin has always regarded Mr. Tyson as
a truthful brand of millionaire but this little yarn
compels it to put on its hat crossways.
Dear Bulletin, —Your par. re Mr. Foster’s
(Melb.) Try Excelsior class is hardly accurate.
Mr. Foster started the “Try” class at his own
house, Canterbury-road, which is in Toorak,
beyond the village, and within 200 yards of the
top of the hill. Mr, Foster’s boys were the lads
of the neighbourhood, some of whom I knew. The
“Excelsior” class was brought into existence by
Mr. Groom, the members of which were newsboys
and poor lads. After some time these two classes
amalgamated and became the “Try Excelsior”
class. Mr. Groom was, I think, a working man.
He was a splendid fellow, a born teacher and dis-
ciplinarian, and won his way into the hearts of all
his boys. Why he eventually left Ido not know.
Perhaps some of your readers could give more in-
formation on the subject. I have always had an
idea he was not treated fairly. His class was the
more important of the two when they united.
There was a good deal in the papers about him at
that time; but, with the exception of one para-
graph some time afterwards, he appears to
have altogether dropped out of notice. — North
Eastern. Chiltern (Yio.), Aug. 10,1894.
Melb. Akgus came out the other morning
with an innocent-looking little paragraph, remind-
ing the printing-trade that the “drag-net” clause
in Treasurer Carter’s evanescent Budget would in-
clude cheap periodicals imported from England by
the ton, so that the trade in Penny Bits and
Waste Scraps and other “ comic ” offal would be
more or less wiped out, to the benefit of local
publishers and compositors. Having, by infer-
ence, congratulated the printing-trade upon this
circumstance, the Argus cheerily observed “it is
not anticipated that the duty will be charged on
the letter class of periodicals, such as the Art
Journal,” which was glad tidings indeed. In
short, the Argus warmly approved of Carter’s
drag-net application to imported prints of the
“ inferior ” class, and when the local newsagents
held an indignation meeting, it' omitted to report
the proceedings. There was a special virtue in
Carter’s net concerning which the companion
organ to the Australasian was mute. The
Bulletin, being an imported, and not a “ better
class ” periodical, would have been subject to a 10
per cent. duty.

The Dead March of the Waters.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
By the blazing messmate seated, as the skies begin
to darken,
And the eager flames ascending seem to flout
the tempest’s din,
Swells, mysterious and mournful over all, a surg*
ing. Hearken!
'Tie the Dead March of the Waters, and the tide
is coming in.
Near the headland where the she-oaks swing their
" melancholy tresses,
And the ti-tree turns to twilight all the sultry
glare of noon,
Where the wrack in clinging clusters every basalt
crag caresses,
Broods in solitary silence Desolation Point
lagoon.
There, when youth’s brief course grows sluggish
and its lures have given over,
Are the merry mountain waters to the bitter
ocean wed,
And the tides roll heavy laden with the sighings
of a lover,
And in secrecy and sorrow does the sea give up
. its dead.
Evermore, from furthest ocean, drift wan garlands
to that wooing,
Bruised and broken, stiff and twisted, are they
cast upon the strand ;
On each face a pallid calmness—half a smile at its
undoing,
Half a shattered hope of rescue —and a clinching
of the hand.
Though I’m ageing to that musio I shall never
cease to fear it,
Still I draw up to the fireside, happy but to be
within;
Think it foolish ? Listen, there now 1 That low
moaning I Gan you hear it ?
'Tis the Dead March of the Waters, and the tide
is coming in.
Aug. 14, ’94.

Weight and dimensions of the regulation
“cat” :
Weight of “ cat,” complete, 90z.; weight of handle,
cloth-covered, 6Joz.; weight of tails, 2Joz. ; number of
laid cord tails, nine; number of knots on each tail, three ;
Length of tails, knotted, 33in. ; Length of handle, 19Jin.
The above is a copy of a recipe for “ cat ” cir-
culated among the Australian provinces by the’Ome
Office. It used to be included in the Governor’s
instructions.
In western Queensland a traveller perished
of thirst last summer. A squatter, passing in his
buggy, saw the body, and, merely satisfying him-
self that life was extinct, went on and informed
tiie police. Two horsemen then came along, and,
after a cursory examination, also went for the
police. Then the police came: they searched
more closely, and found in the dead man’s pocket
a sheet of paper with this scrawled on it: “I am
dying of thirst, and have .£BO on me. I hope
whoever finds me will take it, so that the
traps sha’n’t have it.”
The Anarchists of the Bush.
The distribution of anarchistic literature in
Queensland draws special attention to the con-
dition of that hopeless Australian brotherhood
called travellers, or tramps. For these men the
reign of anarchy has no terrors, and not a few
lustful, revengeful possibilities. There may be
found to-day, in empty houses and sheds from
Sydney to Melbourne, a rough charcoal sketch
representing a female figure with tripod on
head, a torch in her right hand, her left point-
ing back to a house in flames, and underneath
the inscription, “Anarchy is Liberty ! Down
with all tyrants! No law, no law-giver !
Among the travellers this crude drawing is
known as “ The Stiffening Angel.” Upon posts,
gates, deserted houses, and bridges on many
of the inland |roads a white cross is also to be
met, with the motto, “Anarchy is Liberty,
while one mysterious brand is traceable for
over 200 miles along a single line of road.
These things are probably the work of a
few madmen, but that does not lessen the
danger to the society which has made them
mad. The swagman’s usual greeting, “Old
man, it ain’t far off,” may yet possess a more
dread significance than attaches to the voice of
mere idle discontent.
« * •
The swagmen of the past could be easily
divided into two classes—good workmen, who
promptly smashed their cheques at the nearest
pub., and then humped “ bluey ” in honest
search of a fresh job ; and professional “ sun-
downers,” who turned up, year in and year
out, with automatic regularity. With neither
of these have the unemployed of to-day
anything in common. The old swagman
had, in most cases, either innate laziness or
inordinate love of rum to thank for his position,
and the squatter was well repaid for rations
when labour’s own legs brought labour without
cost to capital’s door. To-day all is changed.
Thanks to their own land-hunger and conse-
quent slavery to the banks, nine-tenths of the
squatters have no money to pay for labour;
and farmers have even more than in the past to
rely on their own unaided exertions for putting
in and taking off their crops. Unfortunately,
the number of wage-earners has not decreased
in proportion to the shrinkage in demand.
This winter men, who, a few years ago, had
never to ask twice for a job, are saying in the
bitterness of their hearts, “I’ve never begged
before ; and rather than live this dog’s life I’ll
go to gaol.” When the legitimate bush-worker
is thus tried, it is easy to understand the damn-
able despair of the hordes of artisans, labourers,
clerks, larrikins and social camp-followers
who are now swarming out of the cities
into the poverty-cursed country districts.
Alarmed by the yearly increase in this “army
of the night,” many of the squatters are either
pulling down or neglecting to repair the old
travellers’ huts. From the same cause the
ration has dwindled until a pint of flour is
generally the standard for all New South
Wales, excepting the lower Murrumbidgee and
Darling, where no ‘ ‘ tucker ” of any kind is
given. In Queensland, also, the days are
coming when food and shelter will be denied
altogether —in some cases, doubtless, by men
glad to assert their native meanness on the first
plausible pretext, but in many others for the
simple and unanswerable reason that squatters
who are paying 8 per cent., 9 per cent., and
10 per cent, for money, yet getting no equiva-
lent in the way of stock return, and farmers
who cannot pay their bank interest and store
bills out of the price of their crops, are unable
to feed scores of hungry men for the pure love
of the race.
# * •
During April, May, and June, at one small
holding of 9000 acres on the New South Wales
Southern line, the average of men begging
for food has been 100 per month; and
during the first four days of July, 34
travellers were fed, while at a neighbouring
estate 36 men camped in the hut on a single
night. At another Southern station, once the
home of a big-hearted native who, it is
said, left a provision in his will that no man
was ever to be turned away hungry—two bul-
locks are being slaughtered every week fortravel-
lers, the weekly average of whom has reached
as high as 70. The old cry that men could get
plenty of work if they liked to take it at a rate
of wage which it was in the power of employers
to pay, has to-day no foundation. The writer
was within the past fortnight implored by a
strong young fellow to take him on at ss.
per week, while land which, five years ago,
could not be grubbed for less than .£1 to £2
is now being made ready for the plough at
from 7s. to 17s. 6d. per acre. At the present
time hundreds of men are tramping across
wind-swept plains and over mud-choked roads,
without swags, and owning no earthly posses-
sion save the rags which hang about their half-
starved carcases. Fathers are leaving their
children to the tender mercies of strangers
rather than see them die from exposure and
hunger. Worse—delicate women and half-
grown girls are wandering about without either
food or shelter. In the huts that still remain,
strange companies meet night after night
drawn together by a common wretchedness.
They curse the hand that has doled them out
their pint of flour, and, illogically it may be,
speak of the approaching reign of chaos as a
period which, if not bright with hope, is, at any
rate, lurid with the fiercer fires of revenge.
And this is the spectacle which Australia, with
greater natural wealth than almost any other
country in the world, offers after a hundred
years of settlement!
Queensland Commissioners, who refused a
special train to Charters Towers footballers, ran a
special train to Wallangarra on a Saturday, spent
Sunday discussing matters of interest with the
N.S.W. Commissioners, and returned to Brisbane
on Sunday night. The saintly Ministers of the
Crown, who also raised a hypocritical whine and
stigmatised Sunday football as went
recently to the Bay in the Lucinda, received a
deputation on the Sunday at Southport, and
inspected the breakwater there, making a few
political promises as by-play. Talk about cant!
A Minor Tragedy.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I WAS witness to a tragedy in real life to-day, one
of those that are of such common occurrence
that they only touch our sympathies'in print. It
was in a malodorous by street in a dirty Mel-
bourne suburb. There was a dead_ cat in the
stagnant gutter. A tall, black-haired woman
hung over the shattered verandah-railing of a
small tenement, brandishing her long, bare, tony
arms on high, and cursing at large in a shrill,
piercing voice, inarticulate with drink. All
along the street, on each side for a hundred
yards or so, frowsy, slatternly women stood
in gateways, or thrust their heads out of windows.
Around the tall woman blaspheming under the
verandah was a motley crowd of ragged boys and
girls, capering like little fiends, squealing and
jeering, and inciting the female to further flights
of profanity by their malicious tricks,
I recognised the woman at once as the show
“drunk” of the street—without one of such no
poor locality is complete—the woman whose
periodical “bursts” from one of the few enter-
tainments afforded the residents in a poor neigh-
bourhood.
Every now and again the drunken woman would
make a furious rush at her tormentors, and scatter
them, shrieking, over the road ; then there would
come a chorus of laughter from the frowsy heads
along the street, followed by a cyclone of bad
language. The inebriate cursed everyone in sight,
reviling them with the most opprobrious epithets,
to their evident immense enjoyment. _
Presently an ill-clad, dirty little girl came
hurrying round the corner, carrying a paper-
covered parcel, which she handed to the performer
under the verandah, who quickly stripped off the
paper, and then brandished aloft a bottle of beer,
twirling it in the air defiantly, and yelling her
exultation at her neighbours, interlarded with
insults and curses.
It was her triumph. The women along the
street seemed to feel their disadvantage—they did
not laugh, but a boy in the crowd, seized with an
inspiration, took aim at the bottle with a stone,
and it was shattered in the woman’s hand. Her
taunts were instantly frozen on her lips, her eyes
dilated, and she stood, with her hands aloft, one
clutching the bottle-neck, gazing down at the
spilt beer running over the floor, dumb and dread-
ful. So might a frenzied wife and mother gaze
upon her murdered husband and dead babe.
When I see a tragedy queen capable of that—
that attitude, that gesture, that expression, I will
proclaim a genius.
SILAS SNELL.
“Almost thou persuadest me to be a
Christian ” —as the Jew said to the pork-pie.
Storv from Hoyleton (S.A.). A farmer
noticed'that one of his pigs had been badly bitten
by mice, and he determined to kill it at once to
save as much as possible of the meat. When he
opened the animal he found that the mice had
eaten a hole through its side, devoured all the
intestines, and made nests in the cavity so formed.
Quite a nation of young rodents were caught
inside the pig and killed. The owner of the
grunter tells the story himself, and no other suf-
ferer by the ravages of mice dares to make him
afraid. The severity of the plague may be
judged from the fact that one farmer at Crystal
Brook lost hay worth £75 by the depredations of
the mice.

Off His Shoulders!
A STORY OF SOUTH-EASTERN BORNEO.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Kendall rose to his feet, astounded. Men
said there was no such thing as Fate, yet here, at
Ids feet, was the helpless body of the man he had
sworn a thousand times to immolate at sight!
And now there was no need to stir a finger. lie
1 1 ad only to stand by and let blind circumstance
avenge him.
He tramped away along the sodden sand with
hasty steps, then slower, then a stop to look back.
Around one prostrate form the Dyaks had
gathered striving to revive the spark that made as
yet so vast a difference between it and the other
two stark things that stared, unseeing, at the
morning sun.
“ A minute more of that work,” the white man
muttered, “and there’ll be three of them !” He
turned away his head resolving not to look, and—
in the very resolution—looked again. Then pro-
fessional instinct conquered and he went swiftly
back. The Dyaks—after a fashion that has
“cooked” so many half-drowned men—were
standing the subject on his head “to let the
water run out of his mouth !” Kendall thrust
them aside, got the man over on his back, brought
forward the retracted tongue, and set two of the
Dyaks to semaphore the arms. The rest stood
round respectful of the tuan's wishes, but quietly
incredulous of results, until the castaway’s livid
chest took up the motion of itself. At which
miracle their apathy gave way, and they contended
for the task of rubbing back the circulation.
“Hello! hello!” Shadbolt, the trader, came
down the sandy slope. “ Three of ’em, eh ?—and
life in this here chap, by the looks of him. Here
you are! ” and he held out a battered pewter-
flask.
Kendall took it and sniffed. “Not yet,” he
said. “ But I’ll tell you what you can do. Get
him up to the huts—put hot stones to his stomach
and feet, and, when he opens his eyes ’’—handing
back the flask—“ give him plenty of this with hot
water. Then he’ll do.”
Shadbolt—flask in hand—gaped after the other
as he walked away. “ Leaves it all to me, he
does ! Freezin’ cool kind of a cove is the doctor,
when he likes. Well, it’s Dr. Shadbolt’s case
now, at all events; and he ain’t like the rest of
’em—he can tackle his own physic like a good
’un ! ”
He took a goodly gulp of the arrack and went
over to look at the two defunct. “ Malay, one of
’em,” he muttered ; “and a sturdy little nugget
he is ! But I can’t exac’ly place t’other cove ;
unless he’s one of them Portuguee mixtures from
up Banda way—in which case he ain’t much loss,
anyhow. Now for Number Three ! ”
But Number Three—shoulder-borne—was 50
yards off, on his way to the village.
* * * •
It was mid-day before the trader—much
meandering—reached Ids house. Kendall, flap-
ping at the sandflies, lay in the verandah ham-
mock, and impatiently he turned his away
when he heard the erratic steps. He was in no
humour for fooling, and this man was*Mrunk
again. But Shadbolt’s speech was soberer than
his legs, and his wits less drunk than either. He
stalked solemnly around the hammock.
“ Happy t’report, doctor, that the case you lef’
on my hands is progressin’ favour-a-bably.”
“So are you, I see! I expected as much when
you sent up for more arrack !”
Subsiding upon an empty gambier-box, Mr.
Shadbolt loftily put aside the arrack question.
“Neither here nor there, half-a-gallon of hard-
stuff aint! It’s about these here cas’aways I want
to tell you.”
Kendall sat up.
“ look the reef at Sajang, early this mornin’,
their cruft did ; and in half-an-hour there wasn’t
a stick of her together. These here three coves
made a grand swim of it, but the southerly current
carried ’em down here.”
“Craft?” the other asked. “What sort of a
craft?”
“ Schooner. Banda to Singapore with nutmegs
— nutmegs, no less. And this chap-Carson, his
name is—says he owned vessel and cargo—and
wasn’t insured for a penny.”
“ Good !” and Kendall, jumped out of the ham-
mock. “I’m infernally glad to hear it.”
Shadbolt stopped cutting up tobacco. “The
fellow whose life you saved, I mean; it’s him
that’s the ruined man.”
Kendall wheeled sharply round. “ The fellow
whose life I saved,” he repeated, with a mirthless
chuckle. “ Exactly. Where is he now ?”
“Down at the village,” said the other, staring.
Kendall went inside and came out with his gun.
“Let him stay there,” lie said, “if he’s wise,”
and he marched off into the jungle.
The trader winked a wicked old eye. "Shad-
bolt, my son,” he said, “it strikes me you aint
the only one that’s been at the stingo this
mornin’!”
* * «
The rescued Carson—a sleek-featured,
fair-bearded fellow, with small, deep-set eyes—
was the man to get quietly to the bottom of a
mystery—not to make a fuss over it. So, though
he said little when the Orang-Kaya told him he
wasn’t to go near the trader’s house, he thought
the more. What could it mean if it didn’t mean
gold or diamonds ? “ The tuan's orders ! Well,
I’m a tuan myself, if it comes to that, and I’ll be
in this little swindle, whatever it is, as sure as my
name’s not Carson.’’
• # #
The white men smoked at midnight in the
mellow, firefly-sprinkled darkness, and quoth
Shadbolt: “Yes, I told Dakan to keep the chap
away, and he’s been kep’ away according. But,
d’ye see, Kendall, I think ’twould be only the
square thing to give me a notion of what it all
means, and why you’re afraid of this Carson.
Harmless enough cove he seems.”
Kendall jumped up. “ Afraid ? You’re on the
wrong tack, my good fellow—it’s he who has to
fear me! You’ve heard me speak of Parbury,
haven’t you ?”
“ Parbury ?—yes!—but this chap’s name’s .”
“Never mind names. This is the man !”
The trader puffed for a full minute before he
said: “Well, old man, I’ve sized you up pretty
well since you’ve lived with me, and I know you
ain’t the sort to get your bristles up for a trifle.
And I’ll only ask you this: Which was it—a
woman or money ?
“ Both !” Kendall said quietly, “and reputation
besides! Entire and hopeless ruin at a stroke 1”
“Yet you saved his life.”
“Pooh !—it was the doctor that saved his life,
not the man.”
“ I see. But what’ll the man do now if this
fellow should happen to drop across him ?”
Kendall knocked the ashes out of bis pipe.
1 m going to turn in.” He went half-way to
the door and stopped. “ The scoundrel’s had one
chance,” he said. “Let him stand upon that.”
# • #
Parbury-Carson was prius dementatus. In
his own most evil hap—the fact that Shadbolt
was rattan-classing in the village—he saw a stroke
of luck, as also in the chance that brought two
Dyak boys to blows over a pigeon, and focussed
on the fray the juvenile eyes that had the task of
watching the white man.
Across the yams and plantains— two minutes
hid him in the sugar-cane, and three more found
him on the trader’s verandah. A little blue-back
monkey scolded fiercely at him as he passed
through the first room and peeped into a second.
Then footsteps came along the verandah, and
Carson looked back. A tall figure with features
indistinguishable against the outside glare, stood
outlined in the doorway. The intruder stepped
back a pace or two.
“ I beg your pardon ! ” he was beginning—when
suddenly the other turned and fled.
* * •
“He followed me like a bloodhound,”
said Kendal), late that night. “Followed me. by
Heaven ! as if 1 were the criminal.”
“ Just what he thought you were, too,” Shad-
bolt said. “Somebody keeping dark down here
with a big reward bangin’ to his capture. But
get on, man, and tell me ”——
“There isn’t much to tell. The miserable
wretch kept yelling at me to stop. I had my
revolver with me. I couldn’t keep away the
thought that it ivas to be.”
“Well, out with it!” Shadbolt was hoarse
with excitement; “you dropped him ?”
“I did not. I held on, hoping to keep his
cursed face out of my sight, till I came to the
batang oyer that limestone gorge. You know it?”
“Ugh! don’t I? That stopped him, I’ll bet
my boots.”
“No. When I got across, and turned, there he
was, coming after me. Then the devil got me. I
dodged behind a dracsenia, with the pistol ready,
till he got half-way over. Then I stepped out,
and he knew me, and pulled up with a jerk—and
slipped—and screeched—and ”
“I know,” the trader said. “Them trunks is
like glass, just now. And the Dyaks say that
place has no bottom. Better so, it’s off your
shoulders.”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
Some extracts from an alleged Labour
manifesto circulated among the shearers and bush-
workers of western Queensland :
The Tree of Labour bears fruit only when manured
with the bones of fat usurers, insolent despots, perfidious
politicians, and blacklegs generally. . . . Parliaments
are composed of committees of opulent robbers and
polished thieves, oratorical prostitutes, and abject hire-
lings. ... If squatters oppress you plant raek-a-rock
for their buggies, also fire low and lay them out. If Shy-
locks of the bloody city fleece you, make their homes
desolate. If politicians betray your hopes and sell you
for foeman’s gold, split open their hearts. . . . Study
the science of death. Use bullets, steel, melinite, kero-
sene, phosphorus, flresticks, torpedoes, lithofracteur,
poison, blasting-powder, bombshells—any weapon you
can get hold of. There is no honour in warfare. . . .
Think like heroes, steal like Spartans, lie like hell. . , .
Blast to blazes all human tigers, &c., &c.
Vigorous. It will do at least a little good. It is
a lesson in the noble art of comprehensive con-
densation.
Editor Bulletin,—Potatoes are down to
almost nominal prices—£l to £2 per ton. Why
has nobody tried to turn them into starch ?
Potato starch is as easily made as arrowroot, and
by the same machinery and process, Australian
arrowroot has largely replaced the imported
article, why not Australian starch ?—R.
When Cambridge conferred a cheap degree
on George of York recently, a few undergrads, ob-
jected,_ on the ground that the statute only
authorised the granting of degrees to such British
subjects as were “ of conspicuous merit, or have
done good service to the State or the University.”
York couldn’t prove any good service, but the
learned grovellers gave him his degree all the same.
Michael Gately, when Vic, hangman, made
his will, in which he left all his worldly goods, in-
cluding some specifically-mentioned fees for
flogging and hanging, to Melb. Hospital. Unfor-
tunately, the gallow'srnan didn’t die when he ex-
pected to, and. consequently, no one suffered the
degradation of swallowing medicine paid for with
money obtained from the official man-flayer and
slayer.
A London weekly, apropos to the increas-
ing record of fraudulent solicitors in gaol, exults
over the punishment of a country practitioner
“ who simply got hold of the money of a couple of
clients on various pretexts, and applied it to his
own use.” The result of the little indiscretion
was five years penal servitude -in England. The
Bulletin mentions this circumstance for the
special amusement of certain Australian attorneys,
who, after doing the very same thing, are
“punished” with a few words of mild censure
from a Judge, or at the worst are “suspended”
for a year. Legal embezzlement is no crime in
Victoria, anyway—and yet the blind public go on
drivelling about “the splendid future of the
country,” as though a good climate, territory and
“skite” were the only things necessary to
national prosperity. Why are not the South
American republics fine places for honest men to
thrive in ?
A staid, middle-aged Melb. householder
lately attended a boxing contest for the first time
in his life, and caught the athletic mania so badly
that he immediately invested 30s. in a set of gloves
and invited half-a-dozen cronies to his den next
evening for a few friendly rounds. When five of
them had arrived, and while they were awaiting
the final member, the host and another started a
quiet spar which was suddenly interrupted by a
ring at the house bell. “Here’s Smith !” cried
the householder who, dropping his hand, rushed
to the front door and throwing it quickly open,
dealt the newcomer “one, two,” on the nose
and ribs successively. But the visitor did
not happen to be Smith at all, but a severely
respectable Melb. magnate who is a veritable
Fitzgibbon in the way of dignity, and who so far
fervently refuses to believe that the assault was not
a premeditated outrage, or that the middle-aged
ratepayer was not a cowardly and malicious
scoundrel.

Publications
NOW READY.
SECONTD EDITIOJNT
OF
A Golden Shanty.’’
ALL AGENTS. Price, Is.
1 • Every man can write at least one
book,” every man with brains has at
least one good story to tell; every man
with or without brains moves in a differ-
ent circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and
plainly on one side of the paper only,
obliterating every unnecessary word ;
then mail your work to The Bulletin,
which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competi-
tions the great prizes fall to the news-
paper-proprietor, who gets the guileless
public to work for him for nothing.
Short stories, or ballards, especially on
bush, mining, sporting, social, or dra-
